A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:7==
Abstract
Significance: Necrotizing Enterocolitis (NEC) is a common life-threatening condition
seen in preterm infants, which is associated with a high morbidity and a mortality upto
50%. As of now, the current diagnostic methods of NEC are only able to diagnose later
stages of NEC and can not easily distinguish the condition from other similar presenting
condition like cow milk’s allergy and Spontaneous Intestinal Perforation (SIP).
Aim: In this thesis, a miniaturized fiber optic probe for Optical Coherence Tomography
(OCT) imaging in endoscopy is proposed as a solution to improve the diagnostic chal-
lenges surrounding NEC and potentially in the future become the new golden standard.
The aim of this thesis, is to demonstrate that a design that meets at set of design require-
ments and constraints can be simulated, selected, and fabricated.
Approach: ABCD ray matrix simulations were used to select appropriate probe designs
that met the set design constraints and, based on the results of the pre-experiments, could
be fabricated with the available equipment. The selected simulated fiber optic design was
fabricated with the Fujikura FSM100M ARCMaster splicer, and the size, attenuation, and
beam properties characterized in order to validate the proof of principle. The physical
characterization was obtained with the Fujikura FSM100M ARCMaster splicer and the
Axio Scope A1 Zeiss microscope, and the attenuation of the prototype probes charac-
terized with a photodiode power sensor while connected to the SLD from the system
desribed in [1]. The relevant beam properties was estimated from the Full Width Half
Maximum (FWHM)s obtained from gaussian beam spot profiles acquired from a series of
images of measured beam spots.
Results: The selected simulated probe design for the proof of principle had a ball lens
diameter of 380µm, a Center to splice point length (C-S length) of 0.65mm, and Spot
Diameter (SD) and Working Distance (WD) of 13.1µm and 1503.9µm at λ=830nm. The
characterization of the fabricated prototype probes showed an average ball lens diame-
ter of 398.8±0.4µm (x diameter) and 399.8±0.4µm (y diameter), an average C-S length
of 692.2µm (TL and Mx_Ax), and an average attenuation of 1.010±0.762dB. Prototype
probe# 2 and 3 displayed astigmatism of the beam, while the astigmatism for prototype
probe# 1 and 5 was negligible. The experimental estimation of the SD for the prototype
probes were in general more than 10µm larger than the simulated values, with the varia-
tion in the SD estimates being less than 10µm for prototype probe# 3 and 5, and 20-40µm
for prototype probe#1 and 2. For the WD, the 95% confidence intervals spanned several
hundred µms and only a few of the estimates contained values above 500µm in there
95% confidence interval.
Conclusion: Based on the characterization of the prototype probes, the proof of princi-
ple was partly proven. It was demonstrated that a design could be simulated, selected,
and fabricated with a high degree of precision. Improvements of characterization of the
beam parameters and optimization of the fabrication methods are necessary, before fur-
ther steps towards realization of the concept probe can be made.

xii
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:15==
1
Introduction
Worldwide, it is estimated that, around 13.4 million babies are born before 37 weeks of
gestation (preterm) making them suffer from conditions and/or illnesses associated with
their prematurity, which globally is the leading cause of death in children before the age of
5.[2] One of the common life-threatening conditions among preterm infants is NEC [3, 4],
and have been reported to worldwide affect 1-3 per 1000 life births [3, 5, 6, 7]. With a high
morbidity and a mortality of up to 50% [3, 5, 6, 7] , and 20% of NEC patients dying before
getting surgical treatment[8], earlier detecting and intervention in the disease progression
is crucial to improve the outcome for these patients.
Currently, the clinical challenge surrounding NEC is centered around the current diagnos-
tic methods’ low specificity towards NEC and inability to confidently detect NEC before the
disease have progressed to the late stages. Furthermore, a conclusive diagnosis of NEC
is typically given based on the histopathological findings or the patient’s positive respond
to the medical treatment, which further complicates the diagnostic process, monitoring
and treatment of NEC. 1
Our proposed solution is to use OCT endoscopically to obtain an optical biopsy, which
would be easily comparable to the histopathological findings. OCT is a non-invasive high-
resolution imaging modality, which relies on the scattering of light to create images [9].
With achievable resolution of around 10µm the morphology and microvasculature of the
patient’s intestinal wall can be examined to a degree, which is not possible with the resolu-
tions of the currently used diagnostic methods; 1) planar x-ray ( 1 mm), and 2) ultrasound
(100µm-1mm) [9, 10].
It is believed that this solution has the potential to be the future golden standard in the
diagnostics and monitoring of NEC, and allow for earlier detection and easier distinguish
NEC from other similar presenting conditions. Ultimately resulting in improvements in the
treatment and better prognosis for the patients suffering from this condition. By replacing
planar x-ray as the golden standard, the patients’ exposure to ionizing radiation is sought
reduced.
In this thesis, the first initial steps towards realizing the proposed solution is described.
Taking the small size of the patients and the general lack of medical devices designed
specifically for this group of patients into consideration [11], the proposed design solution
consists of a two-focusing element fiber optic probe. The concept solution and a proposed
design for the fiber optic probe is described in further detail, while the simulation and sub-
sequent fabrication of a simplified design of the probe is used to demonstrate how the
used methods (proof of principle) can be applied to potentially realize the concept design.
The proof of principle consists of 1) the simulation and selection of a probe design based
on a set of chosen design constraints, 2) fabrication of the selected probe design, and 3)
characterization and validation of the fabricated probe.
In addition to this, simulations of the design for concept probe was performed.
1From conversations with the medical professionals at Copenhagen University Hospital (Rigshospitalet)
and Odense University Hospital.
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy

==PAGE:16==
2
Motivation
NEC is a common life-threatening condition among infants [3, 4], which is associated with
a high morbidity and a mortality of 10-50% [3, 5, 6, 7]; however in the most severe cases
it is closer to 100% [3]. More than 90% of NEC cases occur in Preterm Infants and es-
pecially in Preterm Infants with Very Low Birth Weight (VLBW) < 1500g [4, 12, 13]. The
worldwide incident of NEC is reported to be around 1-3 per 1000 life births [3, 12, 13], with
nearly 70% of the cases occuring in Preterm Infants born before 36 weeks of gestation [3].
While NEC only affects 2-5% of all Preterm Infants, it accounts for 8% of the admissions
in the Neonatal Intensive Care Unit (NICU) alone [3].
In addition to the extent and degree of this condition, the current unspecificity and com-
plexity in the diagnostic process of NEC poses a major challenge in the clinic [5]. This
is reflected in the repeated emphasis on the need for earlier detecting and more specific
diagnosis of NEC expressed by the medical professionals 1.
2.1
The clinically background on NEC
The clinical presentation of NEC typically occurs within the first 2-3 weeks after birth in
Preterm Infants [3, 12] and has a peak incidence around 30 and 31 weeks of Postmen-
strual Age (PMA) [4]. The exact cause of NEC is unknown [5, 7], but studies suggest that
it is multi-factorial [3, 4, 6, 12, 13] and there is a bacterial involvement [3, 6, 7, 12].
2.1.1
Pathology
Pathologically, NEC is characterized by inflammation and necrosis of the intestines[3,
4, 12]. NEC can be located anywhere along the Gastrointestinal tract (GI), but the dis-
tal ileum and proximal colon are among the most prevalent locations [4]. Conversely,
the histological findings of NEC is characterized with mucosal edema, hemorrhage, and
transmural necrosis [12]. However, as the histopathological examination is conducted on
tissue obtained from either surgical resection or postmortem, in which the disease has
progressed to the later stage of NEC, the histopathology of NEC in the earliest stages are
unknown [4].
The progression of NEC is thereby mostly described for the later stages of the condition.
Only about a quarter of patients with diagnosed NEC undergoes surgery [8], as surgery is
typically only warranted in cases of either bowel perforation, suspicion of bowel necrosis
or failure to improve with medical treatment [8]. Therefore the current histopathological
findings typically only depicts the last stages of the condition. In the clinic, the progression
of NEC is therefore typically described by the clinical signs, radiological findings, and in
some degree laboratory data, as these are also used in the modified Bell’s staging, which
is used in diagnosing and treatment of NEC.[4]
Besides the physical deterioration of the patient’s condition as the disease progresses,
the progression is also characterized by the following intestinal findings, which is here
listed in order of increased progression; intestinal dilation, intestinal elongation (oblong
and angular), thickening of the intestinal wall, air in the intestinal lumen, pneumatosis, air
1The summarized collectives of the correspondences with the Department of Neonatology at Rigshospi-

2.1.2
Current Diagnostics and Treatment
The clinical presentation of NEC consists of nonspecific symptoms, shared with other
conditions, ranging from apnea, emesis, abdominal distention, abdominal tenderness,
absent bowel signs, and bloody stool to intestinal perforation, peritonitis, sepsis, shock,
and death, while the onset can be insidious or fulminant [4, 14]. The onset and nonspecific
nature of the symptoms makes it difficult to diagnose and distinguish NEC before disease
progression have advanced to the later and more severe stages. Especially, the inability
to distinguish NEC from other common conditions and diseases seen in Preterm Infants,
like e.g. SIP and cow milk’s allergy, easily posed a major challenge in the diagnosis of
NEC 3.
The current gold standard in the diagnosis of NEC is planar X-ray. Though it can be possi-
ble to see thickening of the intestinal wall and dilation of the intestines on a planar X-ray in
the early detectable stages of NEC, Bell’s stage I, the diagnostic value is most related to
the later stages, in which air in the intestinal lumen, pneumatosis, air in the portal system,
and free air, due to perforation, is present. Especially, air in the portal vein is a telltale
sign of NEC, as it indicates that air have crossed over the intestinal wall and into the veins
leading blood from the intestines to the portal vein 4.
However, none of the findings obtained with planar X-ray are direct indications of necro-
sis, ischemia, or inflammation, which are contributing to the cessation of peristalsis and
accumulation of air in the intestinal lumen, intestinal wall, and the portal vein. In addition
to planar X-ray, ultrasound is also used, however to a lesser degree, which is mostly due
to the lack of 24 hour availability. With ultrasound it is possible to see the wall thicken-
ing, perfusion of the intestines, the real time peristaltic or lack thereof, free fluid and focal
collections of fluid, and air/gas. However, the detection of air in ultrasound is associated
with a loss of signal. 5
Currently, the treatment course is based on the Bell’s staging, where stage I and stage II
is managed medically, giving rise to the term ’Medical NEC’, while stage III is managed
with surgical intervention, why it typically is called ’Surgical NEC’ [4, 14]. ’Medical NEC’
is treated by administration of antibiotics, switching to parenteral nutrition and cessation
of oral feeding. In the case of ’Surgical NEC’ a resection of the affected intestines is per-
formed. 6
However, as other conditions can resemble NEC, it is often not possible to know for cer-
tain if the patient really is suffering from NEC. Why a definitive NEC diagnosis is usually
first obtained after either improvement and total recovery after medical treatment or the
histopathologically findings from the surgical resection or a postmortem tissue sample.
In practice, this leads to the course of treatment being decided on the suspicion of NEC.
Unfortunately, this can result in both over-treatment of patients, who do not have NEC,
and delayed treatment of NEC patients. 7

2.2
Considerations regarding preterm infants as patient
group
In general, preterm infants are more vulnerable than infants born at full term. During the
pregnancy the different parts of the body develop and mature at different times. Infants
born before term are therefore less developed and hence less equipped to handle life
outside of the womb. With the preterm infants still undergoing rapid changes as part of
their development, they are more susceptible to changes in their environment with even
smaller changes having the potential to disturb their development or even cause long term
effects.[15]
During their admission to the NICU, a patient is, due to their overall prematurity, typically
treated for more than one condition and sometimes more than one condition at a time. A
patient is typically subjected to several examinations and/or interventions every day. As
stress and pain can lead to rapid deterioration of the patient’s overall condition, decisions
regarding diagnostic procedures and treatment are made with the patient’s added vulner-
ability, compared to other patient groups, in mind. There have been examples of patients
that ceased to breath independently due to the experienced stress from being subjected
to a planar X-ray[15]. 8
2.3
The challenges surrounding NEC
In regards to NEC, there are challenges involving both the diagnostics and the treatments
of NEC, with the main challenge being; that the current diagnostic tools are not able to
easily detect NEC early in the development of the disease and distinguish it from other
diseases and conditions with similar disease presentation like cow milk’s allergy.
In addition to this, examinations with planar X-ray also exposes the Preterm Infants to
ionizing radiation. Though the dose of radiation follows the ALARA principle and for a
planar X-ray in 1 plane is 0.04mSv, which is equivalent to the radiation the patient would
have experienced if it was 4 days and 4 hours older, the patient can be subjected to up
to 30 planar X-rays in relation to NEC alone, during their admission 9. Adding that the
patient can be subjected to up to 10 X-ray examinations daily in general 10, the current
use of X-ray is less than ideal [16].
Regarding the treatment, the over-treatment of non-NEC patients with cessation of oral
feeding and the administration of antibiotics, can delay the maturation of the GI and in-
crease the risk of infections. Likewise, delayed or insufficient treatment of NEC patients
can also severe consequences for NEC patients, like the progression from medical NEC
to surgical NEC. Furthermore, even successful treatment of surgical NEC can result in
morbidity like Short Bowel Syndrome (SBS) [5]. 11

3
Concept
In this thesis, the first initial steps towards a solution to the mentioned challenges are ad-
dressed. This proposed solution being the development of a miniaturized fiber optic probe
for an intended use in endoscopy with OCT. Several potential solutions was investigated
in a special course prior to the thesis, with the proposed endoscopic solution using OCT
being chosen after evaluation and conferral with Lise Aunsholt1. In short, this solution
could provide an ’optical biopsy’ and was preferred, due to its potential for high resolution
images through the intestinal wall along with being more easily comparable to the struc-
tures and findings in the histological staining of the biopsies. With the last mentioned,
also making it easier to validate the solution in the future, which would make it easier to
make it the new gold standard. In the long term, this could also make it easier to validate
future and potentially less invasive solutions. The development of a miniaturized fiber
optic OCT probe for endoscopic use is thereby an important stepping stone to improve
the future diagnosing of NEC.
A schematic of the full concept is shown in Figure 3.1.
Figure 3.1: The concept for the proposed endoscopic solution using OCT. In the illustra-
tion the probe is guided to the patient’s small intestines through the patient’s nasal prone
with a small endoscope. Inside the lumen of the small intestines a balloon is inflated to
stabilize and center the probe, while the intestinal wall is imaged with the side-view probe.
The indicated diameter of the intestine is an estimate obtained from correspondence with
the Pathology Department of Odense University Hospital. The shown OCT B scan in the
upper right corner, is obtained by imaging a sample of the small intestines from a piglet
on the 1310nm Thorlabs benchtop system. The piglet sample was given by courtesy of
Dyrelæge Landbrughøjskolen, University of Copenhagen. The illustration is created in
collaboration with the authors of [1]
1MD, Ph.D. Head of Research, Assoc. Professor (CPN-KU), Consultant/ Chief Physician, Neonatologist
at Copenhagen University Hospital (Rigshospitalet), Department of Neonatal and Pediatric Intensive Care
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
5
==PAGE:20==
There are several ways in which the probe could enter the patient’s intestinal lumen, de-
pending on the patient and which section is of interest. Through discussion with especially
Lise Aunsholt2 and Mark Bremholm Ellebæk3, entry through the patient’s nasal probe,
stomi, and anal channel, have been identified as potential entry points. These entry points
should not require additional surgical intervention, and thereby be less invasive than entry
through e.g. a needle, which typically will require perforation of the intestines [17].
For some cases it might be possible to guide the encapsulated probe through the in-
testines inside the work channel of a small endoscope. However, an other way to ma-
neuver the encapsulated probe might need to be developed to ensure that entry through
small lumenal spaces is possible. This might involve the use of balloon(s), like seen in
[18, 19, 20], where micromotor balloon catheters were used in imaging of swine esophagi
with OCT. The use of balloon catheters have shown increased image coverage [18] and
makes it possible to center the probe inside the lumen during scanning [18, 19] ensuring
consistency in the imaging process.
Additionally, the balloons could also reduce the risk of causing damage to the fragile in-
testinal walls during the procedure, as they due to the larger contact area will cause a
more uniform distribution of the pressure experienced by the intestinal. Furthermore, the
use of balloons could also allow for imaging in areas where the lumen have been con-
stricted or collapsed by gentle dilation of the lumen.
For the scanning, helical pull back with distal scanning is suggested. This would allow
for quick scanning of the intestinal wall, while including the entire circumference. In this
case, distal scanning is preferred over proximal scanning, as it does not directly influence
the size of the probe like it is the case for proximal scanning.[17]
Further specifics on the imaging acquisition and scanning will not be described in this the-
sis, as further investigation concerning this will need to be conducted to ensure a suitable
combination of components and OCT domain is chosen. Due to the general vulnerability
and condition of the patients, it would be preferable if the total time of the procedure were
within the span of minutes.
The focus of this thesis is on the potential development of the fiber optic part of the probe.
3.1
Design requirements and constraints
The most important constraint of the design is the size, which in total should not exceed
2mm in diameter. Ideally, the probe should be able to fit inside a 6Fr nasal probe (outer
diameter 2.3mm). This should ensure that the probe will be suitable for even some of the
smallest and most premature of the patients, both in terms of the size of the entryways and
the diameter of the intestinal lumen. According to the measurements concluded from the
material4 made available by courtesy of the Pathology department of Odense University
Hospital; the diameter of the lumenal space in the ileum was between 2-4mm. Taking the
reference curve for the lumen diameter of small intestines in normal fetal development
[21] into account as well, a size constraint of less than 2mm for the diameter of the probe
will allow the probe to be minimally invasive even for some of the smallest patients.
Due to the general vulnerability of the patient and the related unmaturity of the intestinal
tissue, it is also crucial that the probe has low perturbation to prevent causing damage to
the patient or perforation of the intestines. Hence, the probe should be flexible and easily
maneuverable in addition to the design not containing hard edges or points that can cause
2MD, Ph.D. Head of Research, Assoc. Professor (CPN-KU), Consultant/ Chief Physician, Neonatologist
at Copenhagen University Hospital (Rigshospitalet), Department of Neonatal and Pediatric Intensive Care
3Associate Professor (University of Southern Denmark), Head of Paediatric and IBD Surgery, [Odense
University Hospital, Department of Surgery
4Histological HE stained preparations of ileum resections containing NEC. The sample size consisted of
only four preparations.
6
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:21==
pressure to the intestines.
In order for the probe to have diagnostic value, the resolution should be such that it is
possible to visualize both the morphological structures and air in the intestinal wall. For
the morphology, the required resolution needs to be in the µm range. As the air in the
intestinal wall can have a size anywhere between a few µm and a few mm, the resolution
and field of view needs to be able to cover this span.
Furthermore, the probe should be side-viewing in order to image the intestinal wall. In
this regard, the working distance should be such that the focal point of the probe is placed
within the intestinal wall.
3.2
The probe design
With the main design constraint being the overall size of the probe; it is essential to limit
the size of the fiber optic elements. For single focusing elements using a conventional
Gaussian beam, there is trade-off between the size of the elements, the WD, and the
resolution[17, 22]. With decreasing size it gets more difficult to achieve a high Numerical
Aperture (NA) and conversely high resolution, while a higher resolution typically results in
a shorter WD [17].
However, by using multiple focusing elements it is possible to achieve both a high res-
olution and longer WD, while also maintaining a small probe diameter[22]. Simulations
in [17] have shown that designs containing a Graded Index Fiber (GRIN) lens and a ball
lens, greatly enhances the performance compared to both a GRIN and ball lens probe.
Resulting in improvement of both the working distance and better resolution; and with the
GRIN - ball lens combination it is possible to reduce the size of the ball lens, while still
maintaining the same resolution. [17]
The concept design for the optical part of the probe is shown in Figure 3.2, along with a
capillary encapsulation of the probe. The exact encapsulation, that is most suitable for
the probe, have not been fully determined yet. However, the cylindrical shape of e.g. a
capillary tube will cause astigmatism of the beam, that will have a negative effect on the
imaging performance of the probe [17]. The choice of encapsulation and method thereof,
is important, as the encapsulation is vital for protecting the optical part of the probe - and
should interfere with the performance of the probe as little as possible.
Early on in the conceptualizing of the probe design, the attainability of the design with the
available equipment was taken into account. More specific, it was not possible to create
ball lenses with an angle of approximately 45°with the available equipment. In order to
conserve the side-view of the probe, it was opted to use a prism in the probe design, even
though this would require a longer WD due to the increased length of the beam path [22].
As seen in Figure 3.2, a hypotenuse-coated micro-prism was chosen for the design, taking
inspiration from the miniaturized monolithic ball lens probe with prism design in Singh et.
al [23]. Alternatively, a right angled micro-prism could be used to create the 90°deviation
of the beam path.

Figure 3.2: A cross sectional illustration of the concept design of fiber optic part of the
probe. The monolithic part of the probe is inspired by the GRIN-Ball lens in [17]. The
hypotenuse-coated micro-prism is represented with a grey triangle, while the two rectan-
gles represents the encapsulation. As illustrated, the monolithic probe has two focusing
elements; the GRIN fiber segment, and the ball lens. The first No-core fiber (NCF) seg-
ment is used as a spacer between the Single Mode Fiber (SMF) and GRIN segment, to
enable better focusing of the beam by the GRIN fiber segment.

4
Concept and Proof of Principle
In working towards proving the concept as a whole, an important first step is proving
the principle. The principle being the ability to fabricate a ”simple” fiber optic probe on
the basis of a design that fulfills a set of desired design considerations and constraints.
The proof-of-principle would not only prove that the methods and techniques used in the
fabrication are applicable, but also that future steps moving towards creating fiber op-
tic probes with more complex designs using a similar methodology could be taken, and
thereby closer to proving the aforementioned concept.
4.1
Proof of Principle
The design of the fiber optic probe used to provide the proof of principle was a forward-
looking ball lens probe consisting of a SMF and a NCF segment containing the ball lens.
Based on the accessible equipment and time available for the project, simplifications of
the prior proposed design were necessary, leading to the choice of a simpler and more
achievable design, as shown in Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Schematics of the fiber optic probe design for the concept and the simplified-
concept-derived, proof-of-principle, made to accommodate the available equipment and
time of the project. The simplifications being side-view to forward-view, no encapsulation,
and two-focus optics to one-focus optic.
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
9
==PAGE:24==
From the previous work of Freja Høier it was known that the cleavers available for this
project (Fujikura CT30 and CT50) is unable to provide a precise cleave length for cleave
lengths lower than 2mm. Additionally, an attempt to obtain a cleave length of 1.5mm
had resulted in a 200µm difference, while attempts to obtain even smaller cleave lengths
had yielded in an average of 733µm with a 305µm standard deviation. As the ability to
achieve fiber segments of NCF and GRIN with high precision for lengths lower than 1mm
is crucial for achieving the design described in the concept, the design used for the proof-
of-principle was simplified so the high precision at submilimeter length was no longer
required, resulting in a design consisting solely of a NCF ball lens segment spliced to a
SMF.
Due to the time constraint it was decided to exclude encapsulation from the proof of princi-
ple probe design. This was done as it would not have any added benefit for the conducted
studies and it would increase the difficulty of the fabrication process, which would include
increased risk of error/damage during the fabrication. Additionally, encapsulation would
also introduce a higher degree of astigmatism due to the curved optical transparent sur-
face, which could potentially require a higher level of precision in the fabrication process in
order to ensure the final probe had the desired characteristics, like e.g. working distance
and spot diameter.
Lastly, as the suggested way to obtain a side-view probe was with the use of a right-angle
prism placed in the lumen of the encapsulation, since angled ball lenses were not obtain-
able with the available equipment, further simplifications of the design were necessary,
resulting in opting for a forward-viewing probe design.
In the preceding sections, the proof of principle and reasoning behind the simplifications
of the concept have been described. In the following sections, the methods used to select
a design based on parameters like WD and SD, and the characterization of the equipment
is described along with the results.

5
Probe design simulation
The first part in the process to fabricate a fiber optic probe with a set of desired proper-
ties, is to be able to identify designs that fulfills the related set of design requirements and
select a specific design based on these.
This is why, prior to the fabrication of a probe, simulations of different probe designs were
performed in order to determine valid designs. The simulations were based on ABCD
ray matrices and the scripts were written in Matlab. The simulation scripts were partially
modified/developed from Matlab scripts written by Dirk Lorenser (Feb 2015) and modified
versions of the scripts modified by Freja Høier (Freja Høier, Sep-Nov 2023).
5.1
Method
The simulation scripts uses physical ray matrices and beam parameters as in Kogelnik &
Li [24], Saleh & Teich[25] and Meschede[26], rather than the reduced ray matrix and beam
parameter system as used in Siegman[27]. The propagation of a gaussian beam through
a model of the probe is simulated by the sequential application of ray matrices of optical
elements from input to output, and the calculation of the complex beam parameter q after
each optical element. Which is done by applying the appropriate user defined functions
created by Dirk Lorenser (Feb. 2015).
The complex beam parameter q is calculated with propagate.m from the complex beam
parameter of the input qin and the ray matrix of the optical element M, as seen in eq.
5.1[24].
qout = Aqin + B
Cqin + D
(5.1)
where A, B, C, and D are the elements of the ray matrix M of the optical element, generally
defined as shown in eq. 5.2.[24, 25, 26, 28]
M =

B
C
D
]
(5.2)
The initial complex beam input is given in eq. 5.3:
q = iπ

λ
(5.3)
where mfd and n is the mode field diameter and refractive index of the SMF, respectively,
while λ is the central wavelength of the used laser source.
The ray matrices for each of the optical elements defined in the model obtained from the
dedicated user-defined function created by Dirk Lorenser(Feb. 2015).
The ray matrix describing the beam propagation through a medium is calculated with
ABCD_propagation.m as shown in eq. 5.4[26].

where R is the radius of curvature. For a convex surface R > 0 and for concave surface
R < 0[25]. In the simulations, the curved interfaces in the model are calculated as con-
cave.
The combination and order the ray matrices are implemented in, depend on the optical
elements in the probe model and how they are ordered.
After the complex beam parameter of the last optical element have been calculated, the
properties of the simulated model can be calculated. The simulated SD is defined as
2ω0[25], where ω0 is the beam waist[24], which in the simulations is calculated with beam-
parameters.m using equation 5.8[25];
ω0 =
√
z0λ
πn
(5.8)
where z0 is the Rayleigh range[25] and calculated as the imaginary part of the complex
beam parameter q and n is the refractive index of the medium.
The WD of the simulated model is calculated as −z, with z being the real part of the com-
plex beam parameter, q , calculated by beamparameters.m.
In order to simulate multiple designs of the desired probe model, the main scripts and
probefile scripts from Dirk Lorenser (Feb 2015) and Freja Høier (Sep-Nov 2023) were
modified. First and foremost, for-loops were used to run through the values of the design
parameter, being the length of the fiber segments and the radius of the ball lens. For
each iteration, the SD and WD was saved along with the result of the logical evaluation
the physical realizability of the probe design in allocated matrices.
These matrices was then used to obtain the valid designs, which is defined by the set SD
and WD limits and that the diameter of the propagating beam does not exceed the dimen-
sions of the probe. Lastly, information of the simulation parameters from the probefile
and the valid designs along with their SD and WD was exported and saved in a .xslx-file
automatically. A flow chart of the main script, used in the proof of principle simulations,
with the implemented modifications is shown in Figure 5.1.

5.2
Results
Using the described method, both the design of the Concept and Proof of Principle, shown
in Figure 4.1, was simulated.
5.2.1
Proof-of-principle design(s)
The probe model that was simulated with main_ball_probe_v1.m, see Appendix B.1.1.1,
and Simple_ForwardFacing _ball_lens.m (probefile), see Appendix B.1.2.1 for the proof
of principle probe from Figure 4.1, is shown in Figure 5.2 below.
Figure 5.2: Illustration of the probe model with the simulation parameters L_ball and
L_ballradius in relation to the design of the fiber optic probe shown with the simulated
WD and SD’s relation to the beam and/or beam shape. A comparison of how the probe
model design relates to the ray matrices is shown in Figure A.1 in Appendix A.1.
Simulations for the proof of principle design was made for central wavelengths at both
830nm and 1310nm. In simulations using the 830nm wavelength the SMF was 780HP
(Thorlabs), while it for the 1310nm wavelength was 980HP (Thorlabs).
Furthermore, the SD limits set to be around the Low Numerical Aperture (LNA) and High
Numerical Aperture (HNA) of the benchtop 1310nm Thorlabs system, which in air is 13µm
and 7µm, respectively. For simulations with the LNA and HNA, the SD limits were set to
12µm-14µm and 6µm-8µm, respectively.
In all four cases, the WD limit was set to exclude simulated designs with negative WDs.
The simulation ranges for L_ball and L_ballradius was set to 50µm to 1000µm in steps of
5µm and 75µm to 200µm in steps of 5µm, respectively.
In the following sections, only a subset of the valid designs are shown, as the result of the
simulations had a high amount of valid designs. The full sized data sets are available on
the Biophotonic Group’s Teams channel.
5.2.1.1
830nm LNA
An overview of the simulated designs with SDs closest to 13µm for each valid ball lens
size is shown in Table 5.1. The overview represents 12 out of the 81 valid design for the

5.2.2
Concept design
Simulations of the Concept design from Figure 4.1 was also performed in a similar way
to the simulations for the Proof of Principle design. With the main difference being the
applied probe model shown in Figure 5.4, which contain four design parameters. Why
the simulation script contains 4 for-loops.
Figure 5.4: Illustration of the probe model for the concept probe design, comparing the
probe parameters and the ray matrices. It should be noted that the ray matrices related to
the capillary tube and the sample medium have been omitted from the illustration. Furter-
more, the length of the beam propagation described in ray matrix for mirror2 is the sum
of endfacet_to_mirror and D1_capillary/2.
As the probe model includes encapsulation of the monolithic fiber probe with a capil-
lary tube, the beam parameters for the sagittal and tangential planes are simulated to
account for the astigmatism of the beam.
This is done by splitting the calculation of
the beam parameter q into two; one for the tangential plane, qt, and one for the sagit-
tal plane, qs, at the interface of the encapsulated media and the inner surface of the
capillary tube.
For qt, the interfaces are calculated as planar surface interfaces with
ABCD_Interface.m, while the interfaces qs encounters are calculated with as curved in-
terfaces using ABCD_Curved_Interface.m.
During simulations of the probe design, several observations were made regarding the
18
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:33==
implications of getting a probe design in which the focal point is placed outside of the
probe and the encapsulation. These included that the distance between the end face of
the ball lens and the mirror should be as short as possible. In this regard a distance of
50µm between the ball lens end face and the center of the prism was shown to be ac-
ceptable. Other observations included, that it was difficult to obtain valid probe designs,
in which the focal point was placed at a decent depth into the sample media, when the
refractive index of the encapsulated media was set to that of air, n=1, or that of water,
n=1.33. Setting the refractive index of the encapsulated media to that of a index match-
ing oil, n=1.4527[29], improved the WD. An example of a valid probe design, where the
encapsulated medium’s refractive index set to 1.4527, is shown in Figure 5.5.
Figure 5.5:
The rayplot of one of the simulated valid design of the concept probe
design obtained with main_side_facing_ball_v9_1.m, see Appendix B.1.1.2.
The plot
was made with concept_plot.m, see Appendix B.1.1.3, with λ=800nm, L_NCF=300µm,
L_GRIN=600µm,
L_ball=50,
R_ball=100µm,
L_to_mirror=50µm,
n_sample=1.33,
n_gap=1.4527.
The calculated beam properties for this design is; WDS=497.6µm,
WDT =585.3µm, SDS=14.1µm, and SDT =19.9µm
. It should be noted that L_ball here is defined as the length from the GRIN-NCF
interface and to the first curve of the ball lens, see Figure 5.4.

6
Preparation for fabrication of probe
Prior to an actual fabrication of the prototype probes, a series of experiments were con-
ducted in order to investigate the possible fabrication and characterization methods and,
when necessary, to characterize the equipment.
6.1
Ball lens experiment
Previous work by Freja Høier had shown that ball lenses could be created on a ARCMaster
FSM100P+ splicer (Fujikura) with the FPS.1.9.1 software (Fujikura). It was found that
there was a slight offset between the desired ball lens diameter set in FPS.1.9.1 and the
diameter of the created ball lens, why a calibration curve was measured. As the splicer
available for this project being the ARCMaster FSM100M splicer (Fujikura), a calibration
curve for the ARCMaster FSM100M splicer was measured.
6.1.1
Method
The measured calibration curve consisted of three replicates of each for each of the cho-
sen expected ball lens diameter, which were: 150, 200, 250, 300, 350, and 400µm.
The ARCMaster FSM100M splicer (Fujikura) was connected through its accompanied
usb-cable to a laptop with ’Data Connect For FSM100’, ’FPS 1.9.1’, ’.NET Framework
4.8.1’, and the necessary USB-drivers installed and set up. After the splicer was turned
on, the FPS 1.9.1 application was opened. In the ball lens interface, the ’splice first’ box
was unchecked and the parameters ’Ball Diameter’, ’Adjustment’, and ’Fiber Diameter’
was set to the expected ball lens diameter, 0µm and 125µm, respectively. The returned
’Strip Length’ and ’Estimated time’ was noted.
A 10-20cm piece of a standard SMF (OFS) was placed in a fiber holder (FH-100-250,
Fujikura), 2.5-3cm of the end of the fiber was stripped from coating using the fiber stripper
(JIC-374, Jonard Tools), cleaned carefully with KIMTech wipes and IPA (isopropyl alchol),
and cleaved with either CT30 (Fujikura) or CT50 (Fujikura) to the length of ’Strip Length’.
Before the fiber holder with the cleaved fiber was loaded into the ARCMaster FSM100M
splicer (Fujikura), it was cleaned with KIMTech wipes and IPA, and the ’INITIATE’ button
in the control panel of the FPS 1.9.1 interface was pressed.
The fiber, still in the fiber holder, was then loaded in the ARCMaster FSM100M splicer
(Fujikura) on the right side. After ensuring that a short part of the end of the fiber could
be seen on the splicer’s monitor screen, approximately 2cm on the monitor screen, the
’START’ button in the FPS 1.9.1 control panel was pressed. In the case that no error mes-
sages or warnings appeared, the ’SET’ button on the splicer was pressed. Otherwise, the
’RESET’ button was pressed and the fiber recleaved.
If no error messages or warnings appeared after pressing ’SET’ the first time, then the
’SET’ button was pressed again, whereafter the splicer would start creating the ball lens.
When the splicer finished creating the ball lens, the ’MEASURE’ button in the FPS 1.9.1
control panel was pushed, the splicer-measured x and y diameters were noted and the
’Create Report’ button under the Measurement tap pressed, thereby saving the measure-
ments in an .xslx file.
Lastly, the ball lens fiber piece was removed from the fiber after pressing the ’RESET’
button on the splicer, and stored for potential later use.
20
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:35==
6.1.1.1
Validation of the offset calculation obtained from the linear regression
model
The regression line and 95% confidence interval of the calibration curve for each of the
splicer values, the x and y diameter measured by the splicer, was calculated using regr-
Plot.m. Based on the linear regression equations the offset needed to obtain the desired
ball lens diameter was calculated as the rounded difference between the average of the
output of the inverse functions of the linear regression equations with the desired ball lens
diameter as input and the desired ball lens diameter.
The validation of the equation used for calculating the offset was done by creating three
replicates of ball lenses with a diameter of 150µm, 250µm, and 300µm while setting the
’Adjustment’ to the respectively calculated offset, which is shown in Table 6.1. As a small
increasing trend was observed in the ball lens size of sequential replicates and the round-
ing of the calculated offset, additional two 300µm, three 250µm, and three 150µm ball
lenses were created with the ’Adjustment’ set to the respective calculated offset + 1.
Table 6.1: Overview of the parameter settings in ’FPS 1.9.1’ for the ball lenses segments
used to validate the offset equation.
Ball Diameter [µm]
Splice Offset [µm]

6.1 and 6.2, as a couple of µm is not visually determinable on a scale of 50-450µm.

than the desired ball lens diameter. Potentially, making the tendency of increased ball

problematic.

6.2
Microscope characterization
The initial plan was to use the microscope in the basement of 340 (Axio Scope A1, Zeiss)
to measure and validate the length of the fiber elements in a potential probe. However,
during the imaging of fiber segments created in the ball lens experiment, a discrepancy
in the ball lens diameter measured by the splicer and the microscope was observed. Fur-
thermore, the microscope measured the fiber diameter to be approximately 145-150µm,
which does not correspond to the fiber diameter of 125± 0.7 µm declared by the man-
ufacturer OFS optics[30]. In order to use the microscope for future characterization, a
characterization of the microscope was conducted.
6.2.1
Method
The characterization of the microscope was done by imaging the ball lens fiber segments
from the experiment described in section 6.1 with the Zeiss Axio Scope A1 microscope
in the basement of 340. Due to the lack of dust protecting of the store solution, the ball
lens segment was cleaned carefully with a KIMTech wipe soaked in IPA by placing the
24
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:39==
ball lens on the wipe, folding the wipe in half around the ball lens, and gently pressing the
wipe close around the ball lens, while cautiously pulling the ball lens segment out of the
folded wipe with the tweezers (1369730 3CFR.SA, RS Pro).
In order to image the ball lens segment with the microscope, it was mounted onto the
flat side of a u-shaped metal block (7.5x2.5x1cm), that was wiped clean with KIMTech
wipes and IPA, and secured with tape, as the working distance of the microscope was to
short for the stage to bring into focus without elevation of the sample. With the ball lens
segment mounted, the metal block was secured to the stage of the microscope using the
clasps on the stage and the ball lens segment brought into focus. The imaging of the ball
lens segment was done with the 10x magnification objective, reflective light and the EPI
setting set on the reflector insert.
Adjustment to bring the ball lens segment into the center of the field of view and into focus
was done in the ’live’ mode in the ’Zen2 core v2.5 application on the microscope desig-
nated computer. A image of the ball lens segment was captured by entering the ’Snap’
mode, and the x- and y- diameter of the ball lens was measured along with the fiber diam-
eter with the distance tool, while the an additional measure for the ball lens diameter was
measured with the circle tool. The measurements were noted, and the image containing
the measurement annotations was saved as an .png file, with the filename denoting the
desired ball lens diameter and the replicate no. The ball lens segments were stored after
the experiment.
6.2.2
Results
During the process of preparing the ball lens segments for imaging with the microscope,
some of the ball lens segments were damaged and broke. These being 300_3 and 350_2,
why there are no microscope measurements for these ball lens segments. Furthermore,
no x and y diameter measurements with the distance tool was taken for the 150µm ball
lens segments.
As it was unsure how the FSM100M defines the x and y diameter, a linear regression for
each combination of the FSM100M measured diameters with the microscope measured
x and y diameters was performed. Likewise, linear regressions for the microscope mea-
sured ball lens diameter obtained with the circle tool was performed for each of the ball
lens diameters measured by the FSM100M.
Purely based on the measurements of the ball lens segments with the microscope and
the related splicer values, it was observed that the difference in the FSM100M and mi-
croscope measured diameters was not a constant value, but varied with the size of the
created ball lens.
In general, the linear regressions had a high degree of linearity with the R2s around 0.999.
The calculated slope coefficients were of approximately similar size.
For the microscope measured diameters measured in the y-direction and with the circle
tool, the linearity with the FSM100M measured y diameter was slightly higher than for the
FSM100M measured x diameter. However, for the microscope measured x diameter, the
highest degree of linearity was obtained for the FSM100M measured x diameter. Fur-
thermore, the microscope measured x diameter had lower degrees of linearity compared
with the two other microscope measuring parameters. This could reflect that it was more
difficult to visually determine the x diameter of ball lens compared to the y diameter.
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy

values above 0.3, indicating that the data is not statistically significant. However, the
measured fiber diameter appears to have some dependence with the size of the ball lens.
This dependence might correlate with the observed bending of the uncoated part of the
ball lens segment due to the mounting technique used during imaging.

6.3
Cleaning
During imaging of the front view of the ball lenses from the experiment described in section
6.1, specular reflections were observed. These specular reflection to a degree resembled
’bubbles’ on the surface of the ball lens and occurred in almost all of the created ball
lenses to a varying degree, shape, size, and placement. As these observed ’bubbles’
could potentially effect the beam and beam shape of the ball lens, a experiment was
conducted in order to investigate if it was possible to remove the ’bubbles’
6.3.1
Method
First the ball lens segment was cleaned with KIMTech wipe and IPA, using the method in
section 6.2.1, three times with the KIMTech wipe completely soaked in IPA, to avoid fiber
from the wipe getting on the ball lens. The cleaned ball lens was then mounted to the
cleaned u-shaped metal block with tape, and the side view of the probe was imaged with
the microscope, as described in section 6.2.1, but without measurments of the ball lens
and fiber diameter being taken.
After saving the image of the side view of the ball lens segment, the ball lens segment
was dismounted from the side of the u-shaped metal block using the tape and tweezers
and mounted to the groove on the inner side of the standing u-shaped metal block and
secured with tape, before mounting the u-shaped metal block back on the stage.
The front view of the center and surface of the ball lens was then imaged and the images
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
33
==PAGE:48==
saved as .png files denoted by the ball lens label and whether it was the center or surface
front view. The images was then compared visually with the previously taken side- and
front-view images taken of the specific ball lens segment, in which the ball lens segments
had only been cleaned one time before imaging.
6.3.2
Results
By examination of the ball lens segments from section 6.1.1 with the exception of 300_3,
350_2, and the 150_(1-3), ’bubbles’ were observed on 11 out of 13 of the ball lens seg-
ments’ end faces. However, the ’bubbles’ were not restricted to only the end face of the
ball lens segments, as can be seen in the example shown in Figure 6.11.
(a) 2nd round of cleaning
(b) 3rd round of cleaning
Figure 6.11: The results of cleaning the 250_3 ball lens segment with 2 rounds (a) and
3 rounds (b) of cleaning with KIMTech wipes and IPA. The shown images are cropped
screenshots, the high resolution images are available in BIG Teams channel.
Though some of the dark spots on the ball lens segments were removable after 1 or sev-
eral rounds of cleaning with KIMTech wipes soaked in IPA, it was not possible to remove
all, as shown in Figure 6.11 and 6.12. Indicating, that some of the dark spots on the sur-
face of the ball lens segments could be dust, as it is removable with the applied cleaning
method.
The impurities on the surface that have a higher resembles to bubbles, were not observ-
ably removable by the cleaning method, as clearly shown in Figure 6.12. Indicating that
the ’bubbles’ are most likely located in and not on the surface of the ball lens segments.

between cleaning and the subsequently mount process.
6.4
’Splice First’ characterization
From the simulations done in chapter 5 it was shown that the length of the NCF in the
’simple’ probe design depending on the design should be between 300-1000µm. This
poses a challenge in the fabrication of the probes, if the ball lenses were to be made with
the method described in section 6.1, as it would require 1) being able to obtain precise
lengths below 1mm, and 2) determining how much the fiber length is reduced due to the
creation of the ball lens.
Taken this and the time constraint of the project into account, an experiment to character-
ize the ’Splice First’ option in FPS 1.9.1 for creating ball lenses was conducted.
With the ’Splice First’ option, the ball lenses are created through the process illustrated
in Figure 6.13, by first splicing the two fibers together, then breaking the off part of the
left-loaded fiber by heating and pulling (right-loaded fiber), and lastly heating and feeding
the right-loaded fiber segment towards the electrodes. The last part is the same process
used in both option for creating ball lenses, and utilizes that capillary forces will form a
uniform sphere of the heated material.
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
35
==PAGE:50==
Figure 6.13: Illustration of the automated process for creating ball lenses using the ’Splice
First’ option in FPS 1.9.1, taken from [31].
6.4.1
Method
As the ’Splice First’ option in FPS 1.9.1 have two parameters; ’Ball Diameter’ and ’Dis-
tance to splice’, that needs to be characterized. In addition to this, the ’Splice First’ re-
quires two mode numbers; ’Ball Lensing Mode’ and ’Splicing Mode’. The ’Ball Lensing
Mode’ was set 40 that is the default ball lensing mode and the same mode that was used
in experiment described in section 6.1. For the ’Splicing Mode’ a new splice mode had to
be created, by setting the splice mode to 39, which automatically will set the settings in
the mode to the default. As the electrode gap and electrode height for the ’Splice Mode’
is also used for the ball-lensing, the electrode gap for the ’Splice Mode’ was set to 2.2mm,
as recommended by Fujikura for creating a 400µm ball lens[31].
A series of ball lenses was created with by setting the ’Ball Diameter’ to 150µm, 200µm,
250µm, 300µm, 350µm, 380µm, and 400µm, while varying the ’Distance to splice’ from
0.1mm, 0.35mm,0.5mm, 0.6mm,0.65mm,0.7mm, 0.8mm, and 1mm. ’Adjustment’ and
’Slice Offset’ to 0 .
The ball lens diameter was measured both on the Fujikura ARCMaster FSM100M splicer
and with the microscope, in the same way as described in section 6.2.1, while the C-S
length was measured with the microscope. As it in some cases were hard or not possible
to determine the splice point using the method in section 6.2.1, additional measurements
was taking with the ball lens segment mounted to a glassdish and secured with tape using
both the reflective light (RL) and the transmission light (TL) settings on the microscope.
6.4.1.1
Validation of the estimated offset of ball lens size and C-S length
Validation of the characterization of the ’Splice First’ option was done by creating the ball
lenses with the diameters and C-S length, shown in Table 6.5, by setting the ’Splice Offset’
to the respectively calculated offsets and the ’Adjustment’ to 0, as the offset in the ball
lens size seemed negligible.

eter of 277µm and 278µm. Estimation of the accuracy of the ball lens diameter obtained
with ’Splice First’ was thereby attempted.

6.4.2.3
Validation of the offset calculation
The validation of the estimated offset of the ball lens diameter and C-S length was done
with the ball lens segments described in Table 6.5. As created ball lens diameter with
’Splice First’ were close to the set ’Ball Diameter’ set in ’FPS 1.9.1’, the offset for the ball
lens diameter (’Adjustment’) was set to 0µm. The offset of the C-S length was calculated
from the equation of the linear regression shown in Figure 6.22.
In order to calculate the C-S length from the microscope measurements the equation for
the linear regression shown in Figure 6.3 was used, hereforth denoted Mx_Ax, as it had
the higher degree of linearity of the twp linear regression models for the microscope mea-
sured x diameter, which contained the most uncertainty out of the measuring schemes.
Due to the slight difference in the microscope measurements obtained with the metal block
and reflective light, and the glassdish and transmission light, the C-S length was also cal-
culated by applying the linear regression equation based on the diameter obtained using
the circle tool with both the metal block + reflection light and the glassdish+transmission

7
Choice of probe design for
proof-of-principle
Taking the timeline of the project into account, it was decided to only fabricate one of the
simulated designs from section 5.2. As at least one of the prototype probes were to be
handed over for imaging with the 830nm system, described in [1], it was moreover decided
that the design should be chosen among the simulations with a wavelength of 830nm and
a spot size the LNA of the 1310nm Thorlabs system, being 13µm in air. This would allow
for easier comparison of the acquired images taken with the benchtop system and images
acquired with the probe on the system, described in [1].
Considering the later characterizations and planned testing with the probe, a design with
one of the longer WD was preferred. This was purely from a practical point of view, as all
of the simulated WDs are below 2mm whereby components or potential samples would
need to be placed quite close surface of the end face of the ball lens.
In addition to this, the observed physical limitations to the ball lens segments that could be
created with ’Splice First’ on the FSM100M ARCMaster splicer, was taken into account.
Hence, the valid designs that corresponded to a ’Ball Diameter’ above 380µm and with a
C-S length above 0.7mm was discarded as an option.
Lastly, taking the accuracy of the ’Splice First’ on the FSM100M ARCMaster and the dis-
tribution of the valid designs shown in Figure 5.3 into account, a design with a ball lens
diameter of 380µm and a C-S length of 0.65mm was chosen. As displayed in Table 5.1,
SD=13.1µm and WD=1503.9µm.
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
47
==PAGE:62==
8
Fabrication of probe
In this chapter, the fabrication methods that went into creating the prototype probes are
described. In the following, a NCF ball lens spliced to a SMF is denoted as a precursor
to the prototype probe, while a connectorized precursor is denoted as a prototype probe.
Additionally, the fabrication of the prototype probe for hand-over, denoted as the hand-
over probe, and the fabrication of a patch-cable is also described in this chapter.
8.1
Precursor
Based on the reasons described in Chapter 7, a prototype design of a ball lens diameter
of 380µm and a C-S length of 0.65mm was selected for the fabrication. The precursors
for the prototype probes were created with the FSM100M Fujikura ARCMaster splicer
and the FPS.1.9.1 software using the ’Splice First’ option. Five precursors were created,
stored and labeled in order of creation as probe #1 - #5.
The ’Ball lensing Mode, ’Splicing Mode,Fiber Diameter, ’Adjustment, and ’Splice Offset
was set to 40, 39, 125µm, 0µm and 0.1mm, respectively in FPS 1.9.1, and the resulting
recommended right and left strip lengths were 9mm and 12mm, respectively. After mak-
ing sure that the cleave length of the fibers were set to 9 and 12mm in splice mode 39,
the ’INITIATE button of the control panel was pressed.
A 20-25cm piece of 780HP (Thorlabs) was placed in a fiber holder (FH-100-250), 2.5-3cm
of the end of the fiber was stripped from coating with the fiber stripper (JIC-374, Jonard
Tools), cleaned with KIMTech wipes and IPA, cleaved to 9mm with the CT50 cleaver (Fu-
jikura), cleaned with KIMTech wipes, loaded with the fiber holder into the right side of the
FSM100M splicer (Fujikura), and manually alignment until a ’good’ fiber position was ob-
tained. Likewise, a piece of FG125LA was stripped, cleaned with a KIMTech wipe and
IPA, cleaved to 12mm with the CT50 cleaver (Fujikura), cleaned with a KIMTech wipe and
IPA, loaded into the left side of the FSM100M, and manually aligned.
When both fibers have a ’good’ fiber position, the ’START’ button on the control panel
is pressed. If the cleave angle is below 1°and no warnings appear, then the cleave and
fiber angles for the fibers are noted, and either ’START’ on the control panel or ’SET’ on
the FSM100M splicer is pressed. In the case that no error or warning messages occured,
the ’SET’ button was then pressed again, thereby initiating the process illustrated in Fig-
ure 6.13. After the ball lens had been created the ’Measure’ button in the control panel
was pressed, the measured x- and y- diameters noted, and the measurements saved
by pressing the ’Create Report’ button in the ’Measurement interface in ’FPS 1.9.1. The
precursors was then stored for later physical characterization and used in the further fab-
rication of the prototype probes.
Otherwise, in case the cleave angle and/or cleave shape was not acceptable or any warn-
ing messages appeared, the fiber(s) was restripped, cleaned, cleaved, cleaned, loaded
and aligned.
8.2
Connectorization
Based on the physical characterization, see section 9.1, and subsequently selection of
precursor#1 for the hand-over probe, precursor# 2, 3 and 5, was used in the further fab-
rication of the prototype probes.
Precursor#2 was cut at the proximal end to have a length of approximately 13.5cm, while
the length of precursor # 3 and 5 was not reduced. 1-2.5 cm of coating was then stripped
48
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:63==
from the proximal end with the fiber stripper and then cleaned with a KIMTech wipe and
IPA, before threading the proximal end through the FC/APC connector (Thorlabs), to en-
sure that the fiber fit in the connector.
UV glue (Norland optical adhesive 81) was then added to the barrel of the FC/APC connec-
tor barrel, after the fiber was removed, and the proximal end of the fiber carefully threaded
through the connector. The UV glue was then irradiated with the BlueWave UV-lamp (Dy-
max) for 300ms and then an additional 300ms after rotating the connector approximately
180°to cure the glue. A small amount of the UV glue was then added to the extruding
fiber at the tip of the connector, excess glue carefully wiped away with a KIMTech wipe,
and cured using the BlueWave UV-lamp (Dymax) by irradiating it for 300ms. Using the
manual cleaver, the fiber was then cleaved as closely to the surface of the connector tip
as possible.
The connectorized precursor was then inserted in the FC/APC polisher mount of the pol-
isher machine (KrellTech) and polished using 6µm ((6um, type H, PSA, 50/pk (PF06.0DH-
P-2)), 3µm (3um, PSA, 50/pk (PF03.0D-P-2)), and 1µm (1um, PSA, 50/pk (PF01.0D-P-2))
diamondfilm in the mentioned order. Water was sprayed onto the diamondfilm before us-
ing the film for polishing, and the load/tension was set to ’3’ on the mount of the polishing
machine. The connectorized precursor was polished 3-5 times on the same grading be-
fore cleaning the polished tip with KIMTech wipes and IPA for 6µm and 3µm, while soft
cloth and IPA was used for 1µm, and examining the surface of the connector tip with the
fiberscope (FS200-FC fiberscope, Thorlabs). In the case that cracks or scratches larger
than expected for the used graded film was observed, additional rounds of polished with
the current grading or a courser grading was preformed. Otherwise, if the surface seemed
acceptable for the current-used grading, the tip was then polished with the finer grading.
After the polishing the cap was put on the tip of the connector and the labeled prototype
probe was stored for later characterization.
8.3
Hand-over probe
The hand-over probe was intended to be handed over to the authors of [1] to be used in
experiments with the system described in [1]. In order to minimize the likelihood of dam-
aging or breaking the probe during handling, special additions to the probe design were
made, taking into account the potential handlers’ level of experience with handling fibers.
The most important feature of the design was the addition of a ferrule to protect the end
of the probe and, most crucially, the splice point, which is illustrated in Figure 8.1.
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
49
==PAGE:64==
Figure 8.1: Illustration of the hand-over probe design with a ferrule added to the distal end
of the prototype probe to protect the splice point from damage. Note that the illustration
is not to scale.
As the ferrule, to protect the ball lens and splice point, is unable to slide over the connector
and/or the ball lens, it must be mounted prior to connectorization. Hence, the precursor to
be used for the hand-over probe was selected based on the physical characterization and
subsequently simulation of the resulting SD and WD before the fabrication of the other
prototype probes. Additionally, the ferrule also enables mounting of the probe to a stage
by multiple ways, like using e.g. the SM1FCM (Thorlabs) or tape.
8.3.1
Selection of precursor
Based on the physical characterization of the precursors, described in section 9.1, and
the subsequently simulation of the SD and WD from the measured ball lens diameters
and C-S length, the precursor that had the least degree and amount of ’bubbles’ on the
surface of the end face of the ball lens and was most likely to have a SD between 12 and
14 µm, was selected.
From the visual assessment of the precursors’ splice point, precursor #4 was excluded
from the selection process due to its bubble-like splice defect.
Due to the significant difference in the ball lens diameter and C-S length between the
chosen design and the fabricated precursors, see section 9.1, simulation of the SD and
WD for the fabricated precursors was essential for the selection process. The simulations
were done with man_forward_facing_ball_lens.m by setting the ball lens diameter and
L_ball (C-S length+r_ball) in Simple_ForwardFacing_ball_lens.m.
As it is unsure how the FSM100M defines the x and y diameter, simulations of the SD and
WD was performed for both of the given diameters of the precursor. The simulations were
also performed for each of the different measures of the C-S length, being; using reflective
light with the metal block and calculating the C-S length with the Mx_Ax equation, using
transmissive light with the glass dish and calculating the C-S length with the Mx_Ax, and
using transmissive light with the glass dish and applying the TL_Mx before calculating
the C-S length with the Mx_Ax. For simulations with the C-S length calculated only using
the Mx_Ax calculator, the SD and WD for the 95% confidence interval derived from the
calculator was also simulated.
The results of the simulations are shown in Table 8.1.

Based on the simulations the SD should be between 11.94µm and 14.21µm for precursor#1,

and 14.81µm for precursor#5. Taken this and the assessment of the ’bubbles’ at the ball
lens end face of the precursors, see Figure 9.2, in to account, precursor#1 was selected
for the hand over probe, as it had a low amount of bubbles at the center of the ball lens
end face while also having a high probability of having a SD between 12µm and 14µm.
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
51
==PAGE:66==
8.3.2
Fabrication of hand-over probe
The proximal end of the precursor for probe#1 was threaded through the ceramic ferrule
(CF270-10, Thorlabs) with the indented end face of the ferrule facing towards the distal
end of the precursor. The inner and outer diameter of the ferrule is 270µm and 2.5mm,
respectively.
With the precursor still threaded through the ceramic ferrule, glue (Norland optical adhe-
sive 81) was applied to the fiber between the ferrule and the distal end of the precusor a
couple of cms away from the ball lens to avoid glue getting on the front of the ball lens.
The ferrule was then slid back and forth over the glue to distribute the glue to the inside
of the ferrule. In slow continuous back-and forth movements the ferrule was carefully slid
towards the ball lens, making sure to avoid a build up of glue on the indented end face
of the ferrule being pushed towards the ball lens. After the ferrule had been slid as close
to the ball lens as possible without getting glue on the front face of the ball lens, the glue
close to the ball lens front face was gently removed with KIMTech wipes. The glue was
then cured by irradiating the ferrule and precursor from the distal end of the precursor
using BlueWave UV-lamp (Dymax) for 300s. Glue was then applied to the fiber at the flat
end face of the ferrule and cured by irridiating the ferrule from the proximal end of the
precursor with the BlueWave UV-lamp (Dymax) for 300s.
Aiming for a length of 10cm, the excess length of the precursor was cut off at the proximal
end with the fiber stripper. Approximately 1.5-2cm of the proximal end was then stripped
using the fiber stripper. In order to protect the fiber section between the ferrule and con-
nector, and at the edge of the ferrule and the connector, a rigid plastic tube, which was
able to fit over the ferrule, was attached to the section. The tube was cut to a length in
which it covered part of the ferrule and so it would almost reach the barrel of the later
attached connector. The tube was then slid over the fiber and gently slid over the ferrule
until the tight fit seemed secure. The glue was then applied over the interface of the tube
and ferrule and cured by irradiating the glue with the BlueWave UV lamp (Dymax) in sec-
tions until the entire interface was covered in a sufficiently uniform layer of cured glue.
Glue was added to the barrel of the FC/APC connector (Thorlabs), the proximal end of
the precursor threaded trhough the FC/APC connector, and then irridated with the Blue-
Wave UV-lamp (Dymax) for 300s and then an additional 300s after rotating the connector
approximately 180°to cure the glue. The tube was then brought close to the edge of the
barrel of the connector, whereafter a thick layer of the glue was applied and cured with the
BlueWave UV-lamp (Dymax in section to cover the gap between the tube and the barrel.
A small amount of the glue was then applied to the proximal end face of the FC/APC
connector (Thorlabs). Any excess glue was removed with KIMTech wipes, before the
glue was cured with the BlueWave UV-lamp (Dymax) by irradiating for 300s. The fiber
was then cleaved at the proximal end face of the connector with the manual cleaver by
scratching close to the tip of the FC/APC connector once and then gently taping the fiber.
The proximal end of the FC/APC connector (Thorlabs) was then polished using the pol-
ishing machine with 6µm, 3µm and 1µm diamond film in that order. After each 3 rounds of
polishing the end face was cleaned (KIMTech wipes with IPA for 6µm and 3µm, and Soft
cloth with IPA for 1µm) and checked with the fiberscope to assess the surface to see if it
was possible to proceed. Lastly, light was sent through the finished polished hand-over
probe to assess if any damage had occurred during the fabrication process, whereafter
the cap was put on the connector and the probe was stored.
8.4
Patch-cable
A patch-cable was made with the intention to go along with the hand-over probe to be
used in the 830 nm OCT system described in [1], to better match the dispersion in the
52
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:67==
reference arm to the sample arm, where the hand-over probe (prototype probe #1) is
to be connected. As the majory of the fiber in the hand-over probe is the 780HP SMF
(Thorlabs), the patch-cable was from a piece of 780HP (Thorlabs) from the same batch
that was used in the creation of the hand-over probe.
As the patch-cable should be approximately 8.3 cm, matching the length of the hand-over
probe, a piece of around 20 cm in length was removed from the 780HP SMF (Thorlabs)
coil. The additional initial length is in an attempt to ensure that the final patch-cable after
connectorization will not be too short, as it from prior experience is known that fibers are
prone to snap/break during e.g. stripping of the fiber coating and/or jacket.
First, about 2 cm of one end of the 780HP fiber piece was stripped using the fiber stripper
(JIC-374, Jonard Tools) and cleaned by wiping away the coating debris. The stripped
end was then threaded through the FC/APC connector to check that the strip length was
sufficient, about 1-2 cm of fiber should protrude out of the tip of the connector, and to
make sure that the fiber could fit inside the connector. UV glue (Norland optical adhesive
81) was then added to the barrel, while the fiber was still inside. In order to distribute the
glue throughout the barrel of the connector, the fiber was carefully moved back and forth
inside the connector in upward and downward movements.
The glue was then cured by irradiating it with the BlueWave UV-lamp (Dumax) for first
300s and then another 300s after turning the connector about 180°around its center axis.
A small amount of UV glue (Norland optical adhesive 81) was then added to the fiber
at the tip of the connector and excess glue carefully wiped away with a KIMtech wipe,
along the fiber and away from the tip of the connector. The tip of the connector was then
irradiated for 300s with the BlueWave UV-lamp (Dumax) to cure the glue. Afterwards
the excess fiber at the tip of the connector was cleaved with the manual cleaver before
polishing the tip on the polishing machine (Krelltech) with 6µm, 3µm, and 1µm diamond
film. The surface of the connector tip was checked after each polishing round with the
FS200-FC fiberscope (Thorlabs).
With the cap put on the newly connectorized end of the patch-cable, the other end of the
patch-cable was cut so the patch cable had a length of 9.5cm. The unconnectorized end
of the patch-cable was then stripped gently with a razor blade, as using the fiber stripper
at this step previously had resulted in damaging/breaking of the patch-cable. The stripped
fiber end was then threaded through the connector, making sure that an sufficient amount
of coating had been stripped. After removing the fiber end from the connector, Norland
optical adhesive 61 was applied to the barrel of the connector, as the viscosity of Norland
optical adhesive 61 is lower than the viscosity of Norland optical adhesive 81 and thereby
decreasing the risk of breaking the fiber or an unsuccesful threading of the fiber through
the connector. The UV glue was then cured by irradiating for 300s with the BlueWave
UV-lamp (dymax), rotating the patch-cable and irradiating for another 300s.
In order to ensure that the patch-cable is prepared for handling, Norland optical adhesive
81 was applied around the fiber and the barrels of the connects, as it was not possible
to fit a tube between the connectors to protect the fiber after several failed attempts to
connectorize the second connector, result in shortening of the connectorized fiber. The
UV glue was applied in sections and curred using the BlueWave UV-lamp (Dymax) by
irridating it for around 60s before applying glue to a new section. The glue should be
applied in a thick layer covering both the fiber and at least half of the metal of the barrel.
After making sure that the fiber was sufficiently protected, the UV- glue was added to
the tip of the connector, cured and the fiber at the tip of the connector cleaved with the
manual cleaver. The tip was them polished using the polishing machine (Krelltech) with
6µm, 3µm, and 1µm diamond film. Lastly, light was send through the patch-cable in order
to make sure that it was not damaged or broke during the fabrication process.
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy

9
Characterization of precursors and
prototype probes
9.1
Physical characterization of precursors
9.1.1
Method
The ball lens diameters of the precursors were physically characterized using the FSM100M
splicer and the ’FPS 1.9.1 ’Measurement’ interface, as described in section 6.1.1, before
the precursor was unloaded from the splicer after it was created. The C-S length was char-
acterized on the microscope (Axio Scope A1, Zeiss) using the 10x objective first following
the same method as described in section 6.2.1 with reflective light and the u-shaped metal
block, and then with transmission light and the glassdish.
For the characterization with transmission light, the precursor was cleaned with a KIMTech
wipe soaked in IPA, as previously described, mounted unto the cleaned glassdish and se-
cured with tape, the glassdish mounted onto the microscope stage, and the adjustment to
the placement of the mounted precursor made using the reflective light, as it was difficult
to due the course adjustment with the transmission light. With the mounted precursor in
focus, the light was switched to the transmission light with the POL setting set on the filter
wheel of the microscope. Fine adjustments to the placement of the mounted precursor
was then made and a ’SNAP’ taken. The C-S length was then measured with the distance
tool, the length noted, and the imaged saved as .png.
The front view of the prescusors was characterized in the same manner, as described
in section 6.3, with the microscope, to assess the degree, placement, size and shape of
’bubbles’ on the surface of the ball lens.
9.1.2
Results
As the FSM100M does not measure the quality of the splice, when the ’Splice First’ option
is chosen, it was not possible to get direct measures of the splice quality. The quality of the
splice for the NCF and SMF was therefore only visually investigated with the microscope,
during the measurement of the C-S length.

By only looking at the images obtained with the reflective light in Figure 9.1, it can be
seen that precursor # 5 and 1 have the best splices out of the precursors, as the splice
point is only visible by a thin line. For an ideal splice the splice point should not be visible.
In addition to more visually detectable splice points for precursor #2 and 3, there also
appears to be tapering around the splice points, which could yield in attenuation of the
beam at the splice point. The unideal splices is also visible in the images with transmissive
light, where dark lines are located at the splice point.
Without further investigation, it is unsure what effect the splice defect that appeared during
the fabrication of precursor#4, would have on the beam as it is unknown if secondary
bubble formation solely contains silica or there is an air pocket.
Looking at the core of the precursors SMF segment in Figure 9.1 (b), (d), (f), (h) and (j),
a slight bending of the core away from the center can be observed at the splice point in
precursor#2. This curving of the core away from the centerline of the fiber is not observed
in the rest of the precursors.
From the ball lens end face of the precursors, shown in Figure9.2, it can be seen that all
precursors have the ’bubble’ defects at the surface, with precursor#3 having the highest
amount of ’bubbles’ in addition to have the largest ’bubble’ size observed in the batch.
Precursor#1 have the lowest amount of ’bubbles’ and no visible ’bubbles’ at the center.
Precursor#2 have the second lowest amount of ’bubbles’ and a ’bubble’ occurrence close
to the center, but no ’bubbles’ observed at the center. Precursor #3 have a higher amount
of ’bubbles’, and though the are small in size, a not insignificant amount of them are
located at the center. It is difficult to assess the ’bubbles’ at the surface of precursor#4
due to reflection from the splice defect, giving rise to the onion-like appearance in Figure
9.2(d).
