9.2
Power
9.2.1
Method
The laser current of the SLD was set to 0.150 A, which should correspond to the light from
the SLD being 5mW. Before performing the power measurement of the prototype probe,
the power of the the beam coming from the SLD was measured.
To measure the power of the prototype probe and the source fiber from the SLD was con-
nected with a mating sleeve. Both the end face of the source and the prototype probe was
cleaned with the FCC-7020 (Thorlabs) by moving the end face over the cloth in figure 8
motions.
The power measurements were performed with the power meter (PM100USB, Thorlabs)
and photodiode power sensor (S120VC, 200-1100nm, Thorlabs) connected to a computer
with ’Optical Power Meter Utility’ installed. The ’Operating Wavelength’ was set to 830nm.
The data logging was started, whereafter the ’LD’ of the SLD was turned on and manual
adjustments to the placement of the beam made in order to obtain the highest measure-
ments of the power of the beam as possible. The data logging and the ’LD’ of the SLD
would then be turned off, before either connecting or deconnecting a prototype probe to
the mating sleeve.
The maximum value of each measurements were noted down, and the log-file saved as
58
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:73==
an .xlsx file.

measurements of the source. Note, the y-axis has the unit watt.

measurements, shown in Figure 9.3, using equation 9.1 and 9.2 [32], respectively.

9.3
Beam profiling
9.3.1
Method
The beam profile of the prototype probes were measured with a monochrome microscopy
camera (CS165MU/M, Zelux ®, Thorlabs). The monochrome microscopy camera was
connected to a computer with ’ThorCam’ (Thorlabs) installed through the usb port, and
the operating wavelength set to 830nm.
Using the mating sleeve the source cable from the SLD was connected to the prototype
probe. Both the end face of the source cable and the prototype was cleaned with the the
FCC-7020, as described in section 9.2.1. The prototype probe was then mounted to the
stage (3-Axis MicroBlock ™, MBT616D/M, Thorlabs) and secured in place with tape.
With the SLD turned on the beam was aligned with the camera, the laser current noted
and the distance between the sensor and the ball lens of the prototype probe measured
visually and noted. A picture of the setup with a ruler was taken, before the beam profiling
measurements commended, for later estimation of the distance in ImageJ. The ’Snap’
camera function in ’ThorCam’ was used to capture an image of the beam spot. Adjustment
to the capture time and/or the laser currents could then be made, to beam saturation of
the sensor, and noted. After the adjustments a ’Snap’ image was then taken to capture
the beam spot and the image saved as 16-bit tiff file. Using the course adjustment on the
micrometer, the distance between the sensor and ball lens was then reduced by using the
course adjustment on the micrometer on the stage, as it was not possible to make fine
adjustments with the micrometer on the stage.
The beam saturation and the beam spot position on the sensor was checked before each
image and adjustments were made, before capturing and saving the images.
9.3.1.1
Data analysis
The x and y side profile of the beam spot was analyzed and fitted with a gaussian curve
in ImageJ. To obtain the correct length of the measured beam diameters, the pixel size
was set to the CS165MU/M cameras pixel size, which is 3.45µmx3.45µm[33].
Each of the measured side profiles were saved as an .csv file and important into Mat-
lab, where the FWHM was calculated using grabdata.m, see Appendix B.2.2.3. The SD
at the focal point and the WD was estimated by fitting the series of FWHM calculated
from the beam profile measurements to eq. 9.3, that is adapted from eq. 9.4[34], using
60
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:75==
beamprofile_fit.m (Appendix B.2.2.5).
ω(z −z0) = ω0
√
1 +
(λ(z −z0)
πω2
0
)2
(9.3)
ω(z) = ω0
√
1 +
( λz
πω2
0
)2
(9.4)
In the above equations, ω(z) is the radius of the beam at the distance z-z0 from the beam
waist ω0. Note that z0 here is the WD and is defined as distance from the end face of the
ball lens to the beam waist, while z is the distance from the camera to the end face of the
ball lens.
As the SD in the simulations are 2ω0, the ω0 term in equation 9.4 was divided by two to
estimate the SD and 95% confidence interval directly. Additionally, the FWHM is divided
by 2, as ω(z) is the radius and not the diameter at position z.
The z position for each FWHM was calculated, using retrieveAxis.m, from either the mea-
sured start position in ImageJ, denoted ’analysis’, and the micrometer increments or from
the distance from the protective glass to the sensor, which is 9mm-4.4mm[33], and the
micrometer increments, denoted ’hit’, if the prototype probe hit the surface of the protec-
tive glass in the last measurement.
The start position of the prototype probe was measured visually before the commence-
ment of the beam profiling and in ImageJ. In ImageJ the scale of the image was set by
marking 1cm on the ruler in the image. The distance was measured by visually projecting
the position of the ball lens end face along the side of the groove, and then measuring the
distance to the edge of the groove.

Results

Beam spot shape
Examples of the beam spot of the prototype probes captured with the monochrome mi-
croscope camera are shown in Figure 9.4. The beam spots for prototype probe #1 and

Figure 9.4: The beam spot of the last image for each beam profile measurement. The

Besides from the beam spots, some of the artifacts from the camera is also present in
the images shown in Figure 9.4. During the image acquisition it was observed that the
camera contained a significant amount of these artifacts, and that certain regions of the
areas of the camera sensor was more affected than others. These artifacts consists of cir-
cular dark spots with a surrounding ringing pattern. As the beam spots were captured on
different parts of the camera sensor during the image acquisition the effect of the artifacts
on the captured beam spot varies between images. The inconsistency in the placement
of the beam spot on the camera sensor, was mostly a result of the mounting of the proto-
type probes, in which the end of the prototype probes were not fixed, except for prototype
probe#1 and #5. Allowing the end of the prototype probes to be angled and a certain de-
gree of movement, which was both unwanted. Prototype probe #5 was fixed to the ruler
due to the length of the prototype probe, so the observed variation of the placement of the
beam spot on the camera sensor was less than for the other probes both no completely
insignificant, as the ruler had some give. The tubing of prototype probe #1 made it quite
stable, but also caused the probe to curve, and do to the shape of the prototype probe,
not making it possible to mount it straightly.
9.3.2.2
Gaussian fitting of the beam
In general the beam spot profiles fitted decently to the gaussian curve generated by Im-
ageJ. However, saturation and the ringing effect from the sensor artifacts effected the fit in
some of the beam spot profiles. Examples of the obtained gaussian fits for beam profiles
with a good gaussian profile, ringing effects, and saturation are shown in the Figure 9.5,
9.6, and 9.7.

As seen in Table A.2 the estimated SDs are significantly larger than the 11-15µm that was
simulated based on the physical characterization. Likewise, the estimated WDs are quite
far away from the the simulated 1500-1700µm WD based on the physical characterization.
Furthermore, the estimated WDs are negative, except for prototype probe #2 experiment
2, prototype probe #3 experiment 3, and prototype #5 experiment 2, which would indicate
that the focal point of the probe would be inside the SMF segment of the probe. In addition
to this, only the results where ’hit’ was used to calculate the positions of the images, had
95% confidence intervals that contained WDs above 500µm.
Based on the results in Table 9.3 there seems to be a small difference in the x and y
estimation in the individual experiments for prototype probe #1 and #5, while the difference
in general is larger for the experiments for prototype #2 and #3. The difference in the
results of the estimations also varies from prototype probe. For prototype probe #3 and
#5 the variation in the estimations of the SD is less than 10µm, while it for prototype probe
#1 and #2 closer to 20-40µm.

==PAGE:84==
10
Discussion
In general the fabricated prototype probes where quite similar in regards to the ball lens
size and the C-S length, with the average x and y diameter of the ball lenses being
398.8±0.4µm and 399.8±0.4µm, while the measured average C-S length for the probe is
689.0µm, 692.2µm, and 689.3µm for the measuring techniques respectively. Compared
to the simulated design with a ball lens size of 380µm and a C-S length of 650µm, the
diameters and C-S length of the prototype probes are approximately 19µm and 39-42µm,
respectively. Simulation of the beam properties based on the physical characterization of
the precursors showed that, despite this difference, the SDs and WDs for the prototype
probes would be between 11-15µm and approximately between 1500-1700µm, respec-
tively.
However, the estimated SD of the prototype probes based on the measurements from
the beam profiling was significantly larger than the corresponding simulated SDs. Fur-
thermore, the estimated WDs for the prototype probes were generally quite far away from
the simulated WDs, with most of the estimated WDs being of negative value and only a
few possessing a 95% close to or containing values within the span of the simulated ones.
In this regard, it should be noted that only WDs estimated using the ’hit’ calculation of the
image positions in the beam profiling displayed WDs containing a positive value above
500µm in the 95% confidence interval.
As it was not possible to measure the beam waist directly, due to the distance from the
protective glass and to the sensor of the CMOS camera is larger than even the largest
estimate of WD for each of the prototype probes, the SD and WD needed to be estimated.
Furthermore, looking at the calculated axis for the position of the images during the beam
profiling, it can be seen that the position of some of the images appears to be placed
behind the protective glass, which clearly does not reflect the actual position of the probe
during the beam profiling. This will of course affect the estimation of the beam properties,
and most likely result in SD being estimated as larger than the actual value.
There are quite a few factors contributing to the incorrect calculation of the probe position.
These includes the uncertainty in determining the start position of the probe in relation to
the sensor. This accounts for both the visual determination of the distance with the ruler,
noted as ’Experimental’, and the distance determined by analysis of a picture of the setup
for the first image in the beamp profiling in ImageJ, noted ’ananlysis’. For the experimen-
tal determination, this is due to it being difficult to estimate where the sensor is compared
to the rest of the CMOS camera and with accuracy visually determine the actual position
of the end of the ball lens with the ruler. In the case of the analysis with ImageJ, it is
likewise, difficult to determine the actual distance between the ball lens and the sensor.
To complicate the situation even further, the images were taken at slight angle, in order to
be able to see the ball lens as the start position, due to the mac range of the micrometer
stage, often was placed inside the casing of the CMOS camera. This angle in the image,
makes it difficult to measure the length and also correctly project the ball lens placement
on to the shown side of the camera. Furthermore, the angle is unknown and most likely
vary from image to image.
For the beam properties estimated with an image position axis calculated with ’hit’, it is
also hard to determine the correct image position axis, as it was hard to know exactly
when the ball lens touched the protective glass. Why the ball lens could have touched
the protective glass before the last image being taken in the experiment, which also will
cause the SD to be estimated as larger than the actual size. This especially looks to be
70
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:85==
the case for prototype probe# 1 in experiment 3.
Another factor affecting the calculation of the image position axis, it the assumed incre-
ment in the position between measurements, which it obtained by the adjustment of the
micrometer on the stage, as only the coarse adjustment worked. Even though, it was dif-
ficult to determine if the stage actual moved the desired amount, as the adjustment knob
was also a bit loose. This could result in the assumed distance between the ball lens
position in consecutive images being unknown and smaller than assumed. In relation to
the estimation of the SD this would also increase the size compared to the actual size, as
the beam spot of a more dispersed part of the beam will be assumed to be closer to the
sensor than it actual is.
Furtermore, the CMOS camera was sometimes accidentally moved during the acquisition
of the beam profile, which will also cause an potentially incorrect calculation of the posi-
tion of the ball lens for the following images. In this case however, the actual position of
the ball lens in the images could be both closer to or further away from the sensor than
assumed.
The way the prototype probes where mounted to the stage, could also affect the calcu-
lation of the position of the ball lens, as it was not possible to have the prototype probes
being straightly secured. For prototype probe # 1 this was caused by the tube curving the
prototype probe, which caused the aim at the sensor being angled and even differ during
the acquisition, as the prototype probe was moved closer to the CMOS camera. In the
cases for the other prototype probes, this angled aim towards the sensor was more down-
ward, as it was caused by gravity due to the length of the prototype probes. However, in
all cases the angled aim could cause the beam spot to less symmetric and more diffuse
due to the angle, which would result in the calculated FWHM being larger than if there
was not angle.
Moreover, the fitting of the beam spot profile to the gaussian curve was also sometimes
observed to result in the FWHM being larger as the maximum of the fitted profile typically
was lower than the actual maximum of the measured beam spot profile. However, more
critical was the effect the camera artifacts has on the beam spot profile, as the ringing
often caused widening of the beam spot profile in addition to causing a worse gaussian
fit, when comparing the beam spot with beam spot profiles with a lower degree of camera
artifacts. This will also result in the estimated SD being larger than the actual SD of the
prototype probe.
Looking at the results of the estimated WD it would appear that the incorrect calculated
distance affect the estimation of the WD to a high degree. This notion is based both on
the large span of the 95% confidence interval of the estimation along with the negative
value of the WD, which if it was correct would suggest that the focal point is located inside
of the probe. Taking the actual design of the prototype probes in to considerations, this
does not seem plausible.
Considering all this, the estimated SD seems reasonable close to the simulated values
for the prototype probes. However, it is not possible to directly validate the fabrication of
the simulated proof of principle design based on the beam profiling, it is still possible to
say that the actual size of the beam spots most likely is smaller than the estimated values.
Though, the estimated SD and the WD of the prototype probes is not close to the simu-
lated values, some of the estimated SD and WD are comparable to the SD and WD of
needle based microendoscopes [35, 36, 37].
It should also be noted, that this difference in the estimated SD and WD could also be
that simulated values does not correctly resemble the actual SD and WD of the prototype
probes. This would be related to the physical characterization of the prototype probes
along with the actual fabrication process like e.g. the formation of the ’bubbles’ on the ball
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
71
==PAGE:86==
lens surface, which could change the actual beam properties of the prototype probes. In
order to estimate the exact effect of this, it will be necessary to first improve the method
for the beam profiling.
Improvements to the current setup like e.g. a more accurate micrometer stage and us-
ing the surface of the protective glass to calibrate the axis for the position of the image
acquisition. Considering the issues related to the currently used method for mounting
the prototype probes to the micrometer stage, it would be desirable to opt for a different
mounting technique, which would ensure a straight aiming at the camera and prevent
easy movement of the prototype probes. A relay lens or system of relay lenses could also
be implemented in the setup to be able to measure the beam waist more directly with the
CMOS camera [38]. However, it should be noted that replicating the beam profiling with
the current CMOS camera, which should also be considered replaced due to its artifacts,
will be difficult, as it was only possible not the saturate the sensor due to issues with the
end face of the source patch cable of the SLD. This issue caused the power coming from
the patch cable to be significantly lower than it should be, which is also the reason why
more power measurements of the prototype probes was not performed. Additionally, to
be able to exclude the effect of dust on the measurements, a better method to clean the
ball lens end faces of the prototype probes should be implemented.
It should be noted, that based on the experiment with cleaning the ball lens end faces, it
was not possible to remove the ’bubbles’ with IPA and KIMTech wipes. From correspon-
dence with Sameen Salem1 it was suggested that the ’bubbles’ could be caused by debris
from the electrodes, which would agree with the increased accumulating of white material
on the FSM100M’s electrodes throughout the project, and that it maybe could be possible
to remove them using an ultrasonic bath. This was not attempted in this thesis, but should
be attempted in order to be able to truly confirm, that the ’bubbles’ are not located on the
surface of the ball lenses.
Due to the unintended rotation of the ball lens segments in relation to the cleaning pro-
cess and mounting for the imaging in the experiment regarding the effect of cleaning, it
is difficult to evaluate the actual effect of the employed cleaning method. The difference
in the focus and microscope settings for the images used to evaluate the effect of clean-
ing, is also not optimal in a comparison setting. Why the actual effect of cleaning the ball
lenses with IPA and KIMTech wipes can not be easily estimated. Moreover, taking the
experienced risk of cleaning the ball lenses in this way and that the ball lenses are easily
introduced to dust with the current storage method, a different cleaning method and/or
storage method might be considered.
Comparing the observed shape of prototype probes beam spots with the power measure-
ments, it can be seen that the prototype probes with strongly visible astigmatism also
appears to have higher attenuation. This most likely is due to the degree, size, and place-
ment of the ’bubbles’ on the ball lens end face, which also correlates with probe# 3 having
the highest measured attenuation of 1.891dB and also being the probe with the highest
amount and largest size of ’bubbles’. However, both prototype probe# 2 and 3 also have
the visually determined worst splice quality out of the four prototype probes, which can
also contribute to the attenuation. As the measured optical power of the SLD before each
power measurement is 7.392±0.06dBm, this does not appear to have a significant effect
on the measurements. As the voltage of the SLD was observed to be constant during the
measurements, this specific difference it most likely an expression of the inconsistency in
aiming the beam at the power meter sensor, which can also be observed in the variation
1Business Development Engineer (Specialty optical Products at Fujikura.
72
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:87==
in the measured mW at the peaks for each measurement in Figure 9.3.
On the contrary, the coupling efficiency between the prototype probes and the SLD’s
source patch cable could have a significant effect, as an significant difference of about
1mW was observed in relation to the initial testing of the beam profiling setup and in the
characterization of prototype probe# 1. The difference in attenuation could, however, also
be due to insufficient cleaning of the end face of the two mentioned probes, as these were
experienced to be more difficult to clean compared to prototype probe# 1 and 5.
As only one power measurement was conducted for the prototype probes, it is not pos-
sible to determine the exact significance that the cleaning of the ball lens end face or the
coupling efficiency had, why more measurements should be performed. In order to be
able to evaluate the effect of the ’bubbles’ on the end face of the ball lens have on the
attenuation, an additional characterization test, where the loss at the splice point is esti-
mated, should be conducted. It might be possible to perform this test with the FSM100M
ARCMaster splicer, if a custom splice mode protocol was created in FPS 1.9.1, where the
loss is measured right after the fibers are spliced together.
Based on the splice point evaluation with the FSM100M ARCMaster splicer done with the
default splice mode, the loss at the splice point should be less than 0.2dB. Under the as-
sumption, that loss at splice point has an significant effect on the overall attenuation of the
prototype probe, the attenuation could be lowered by optimizing the splice procedure for
the fiber. By doing this, it might also be possible to avoid the observed bending/curving of
the core of the SMF, which might also be a large contributor to the measured attenuation.
It should be noted, that for with the design of the prototype probes this phenomena might
be less problematic, but that it for the concept design could be detrimental, as the core of
the SMF and the GRIN fiber should be aligned.
Further optimizations can also be made regarding the ball lens size and C-S length. This
notion, is based on the observation of especially the change in accuracy of the ball lens
size obtained with ’Splice First’, as seen when comparing the results of section 6.4.2.1
and 6.4.2.3. The change could be due to the apparent accumulation of the debris on the
FSM100M ARCMaster splicer electrodes, which was observed to gradually increase dur-
ing the period of the project.
However, this observed change could also be due to limited number of experiments per-
formed, from which the calculations of the offsets were estimated. To investigate this,
more experiments should be conducted to obtain at least 3 replicates for each point. Ad-
ditional experiments, should also be conducted in order to investigate if the current applied
assumption that Ball Diameter and Distance to splice point are independent is reason-
able. Purely based on the method employed in ’Splice First’, it would stand to reason that
the two parameters are not entirely independent.
In regards to the C-S length, further comparison of how the imaging technique using po-
larized transmissive light with the glass dish and reflective light is needed in order to better
determine how the C-S length should be calculated, when using the 10x objective on the
microscope. As the technique using the polarized light is better at determining the splice
point, even with good quality splices, it is crucial to be able to calculate the C-S length
correctly if further optimization of the achieved C-S length on the FSM100M ARCMaster
splicer is to be made.
However, the calculations to estimate the C-S length is obtained from the characterization
of the microscope, why the use of these should be discussed briefly. Overall, the equa-
tions display a high level of linearity and are similar the calculated length differing with a
few µms for the equations. When taken the results from Figure 6.9 and 6.10 into account,
the measured length of the fiber diameter seems to be slightly dependent on the size of
the ball lens diameter. As previously mentioned, this dependence seems to correlate with
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
73
==PAGE:88==
the observed bending in the uncoated part of the ball lens segment while mounted. This
bending of the fiber will affect the microscope measurements, as it causes the focal plane
of the central axis of the probe along the optical path to be nonparallel, and thereby in-
troducing a not yet accounted for term in the equation for characterizing the microscope.
Experiments to estimate this effect could be conducted. Another approach to avoid this,
could be to employ a mounting in which the central axis of the fiber segment is level and
parallel to the focal plane of the microscope.
As the calculations to estimate the lengths measured with the microscope is based on the
ball lens segments from the ball lens experiment, one should beware of that it is assumed
that the actual size of the x and y diameter is the ones measured by the FSM100M, why the
are a so-called ground truth. This assumption could potentially be incorrect, and the ball
lenses could thereby have an actual size that deviates from the assumed value. Further-
more, it is not known how the x and y diameter is defined by FPS 1.9.1, why these might
not correlate entirely with the definition of the x and y diameter measured with the micro-
scope. Likewise, the actual precision and accuracy of the x and y diameter measured with
the FSM100M are also unknown. Though, they are still assumed to be superior to that of
the microscope, based on the observed discrepancy in the fiber diameter declared by the
manufactor and the one meassured with the 10x objective on the microscope along with
the FSM100M’s intended use for high precision splicing[39]. In this regard, however, it is
also unknown if slight difference in the x and y diameter measured on the FSM100M is an
actual difference or more a reflection of the employed measuring technique and definition
of the two diameters.
Furthermore, it is important to be aware that ball lens segments was made with the OFS
fiber and not the FG125LA. Though the fiber diameter should be similar, the actual size
of the fibers might be slightly different and thereby affecting the estimation slightly.
Additionally, as the OFS is a SMF and FG125LA is NCF, there are most likely also an
difference in the density of the fiber, which would effect the fabrication of the ball lens.
Why it should be noted, that the equations to calculate the offset is based on the OFS
ball lens segments, and that there could be a slight difference in the offset needed in the
fabrication of a ball lens with the OFS and the FG125LA.
As the validation of the proof of principle leaves some room for discussion, it is in return
also not possible to directly validate the simulations either. Suggestions, based on the
measured beam properties of the prototype probes, in regards to the improvement of the
simulation would therefore not be of much value, why these will not be mentioned. How-
ever, suggestions on how to improve the simulation based on the results of the simulations
of the probe design, will be given instead.
Looking, at especially the results for the 1310nm HNA probe design, it would appear that
simulation range of L_ball could be increased, which should result in valid designs with
larger WD. In general the simulation ranges for the probe model parameter could be in-
creased, while the step size could be decreased, as this would allow for simulation of more
probe designs. In this regard, it could be possible to better determine the optimized the
beam properties for a specific ball lens size, as the simulated beam properties of the valid
probe designs for the proof of principle displayed an asymmetric converging behavior to-
wards an optimal point. In relation to the simulation of the concept probe design, this will
be even more important, as the currently used step size is 50µm. Comparing this to the
step size of 5µm used in simulations of the proof of principle probe design, this decrease
in step size could be essential to obtaining a more suitable probe design to be used in the
74
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:89==
realization of the concept probe.
In addition to this, changes to the simulations of the concept design could also be made re-
garding the encapsulation. As the exact encapsulation method have not been decided yet
and there might be opted for choice of encapsulation that does not result in astigmatism
of the beam, the simulated encapsulation should resemble the choice of encapsulation.
However, as astigmatism can also be limited by utilizing a elliptical ball lens, changes can
also be made to make the simulation resemble the effect an elliptical ball lens would have
on the beam.
For better selection of a suitable probe design, including additional beam properties like
e.g. the Rayleigh length could be beneficial. As of now, some of the simulation results
had several hundreds valid probe designs. With the addition of more validation parame-
ters, it would be easier to compare the designs and their imaging quality.
Lastly, it should be mentioned that the simulated probe designs for the prototype probes
made with air as the sample media. This choice was made, to make it easier to compare
the simulated beam properties with the characterization test, which was performed in free
space. Why, if the prototype probes, were to be used for imaging of an actual sample,
the beam properties would be different due to the change in refractive index of the media.
This have not been investigated in this thesis.
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
75
==PAGE:90==
11
Future steps
Several steps need to be taken, before a fully functional probe, that is able to fulfill the
current needs for earlier and more specific diagnosis of NEC, can be realized. In the
long run this includes, figuring out the specifics of the technical aspects of the scanning,
guidance and steering of the probe, and potential inclusion of a balloon catheter in the
design. Additionally, in vitro and in vivo studies also needs to be conducted, both in re-
gards to set up better design constraints and requirements and in order to be able to get
the probe approved as a medical device suitable for use in the clinic in the future. These
studies both needs to include testing of the probe and be able to validate that the probe
is suitable/useful in the diagnosis of NEC. For the in vitro and in vivo tests in animals,
the Department of Veterinary and Animal Studies at University of Copenhagen, which are
acknowledged worldwide for their NEC model in piglets, have expressed their interest in
collaboration in the conduction of potential future studies.
Likewise, the collaborators from Rigshospitalet and Odense University Hospital in this
project, have also expressed a desire for potential future collaborations within their re-
spective capacities. However, all of the above will most likely be several years in the
future.
As of now, the initial steps are to realize the concept design of the probe. It has already
been demonstrated in this thesis, that it is possible to simulate the concept design of the
fiber optic parts of the probe, and the realization will therefore be related to the selection
of appropriate designs and the fabrication hereof. In this regard, being able to fabricate
the monolithic part of the probe with high accuracy and precision is crucial. Especially,
the current inability to achieve the shorter lengths of the fiber segments in the probe de-
sign, needs to be mitigated. One solution could be to use a micrometer stage to adjust
the placement of the fiber in the cleaver. A concept of this solution is shown in Figure
11.1, and will most likely involve 3D printing components to easily secure the fiber to the
micrometer stage.
76
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:91==
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
77
==PAGE:92==
Figure 11.1: Concept illustration of the proposed solution to be able to fabricate probes
containing fiber segments with lengths >2mm. A micrometer stage with a 3D printed fiber
holder is used to move the fusion spliced fiber a desired length in relation to the known
strip length before cleaving is performed. For the intermediate fiber segments, step 2) to
3) can be repeated in concession.
Another important step to realize the concept probe design, is encapsulation of the probe.
The encapsulation will be crucial in both the later approval process of the probe and in
general to protect the monolithic part of the probe. In this regard, several factors needs to
be taken into consideration. Assuming that a prism or mirror is still preferred to obtain a
side-viewing probe and that index matching is required to achieve an appropriate working
distance outside of the probe, the chosen encapsulation method will need to allow: 1) the
mounting of the prism and 2) the insertion of the index matching oil. However, it might be
beneficial to opt for an angled ball lens instead of a prism, as the required WD to obtain a
focal point outside of the probe would be shorter. Though, this will require the equipment,
that have not been available during this project.
Considerations regarding the shape of the encapsulation will also need to be taken into
account, as encapsulation methods like e.g. capillary encapsulation due to the shape of
capillary cause astigmatism in side-view probes[22]. In the case that an encapsulation
method with a cylindrical shape is chosen, opting for an elliptical ball lens should be con-
78
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
==PAGE:93==
sidered in order to attempt to negate the astigmatism caused by the encapsulation[22].
This would require slight changes to the simulation code to accommodate for the ellipti-
cal shape of the ball lens or the potential use of postprocessing of the image acquisition
in order to improve the image quality. As the currently used fusion splicer (FSM100M
ARCMaster, Fujikura) does not allow for shaping of the ball lens, using the LZM-100 LAZ-
ERMaster from fujikura or equivalent equipment should be considered. An added benefit
of opting for the CO2 laser splicer is that, according to correspondence with Fujikura1, it
would be possible to avoid the observed ’bubble’ defects on the surface of the ball lenses.
In relation to the development of an early prototype of the concept probe, a distal rotary
joint with pull back could be used to obtain a helical scan pattern.[17]
Even though the development of the concept design of the probe have been focused
on its potential use in the diagnostic of NEC, it should be noted that there are other po-
tential uses for the probe. This including potential uses related to the diagnostic and/or
treatment of other diseases of conditions, and also for other patient groups, as the small
size could make it possible to image, where the current size of e.g. endoscopes prohibit
it today.
A non-diagnostic focused potential use could be in vitro and/or in vivo animal studies.
With the right beam properties of the probe’s output beam the probe could be used to
study drug delivery using OCT[40]. Furthermore, the probe could also be used in imaging
with other optical image modalities.

at Fujikura
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
79
==PAGE:94==
12
Conclusion
In this thesis it has been shown that it is possible to simulate and eventually select a two
focusing-element fiber optic probe that will meet the design considerations, requirements,
and constraints for a miniaturized fiber optic probe for OCT imaging in endoscopy for
potentially earlier and more specific diagnosis of NEC in preterm infants.
The proof of principle was partially proven, as a simulated design for the prototype probes
was selected and fabricated with the physical characterization showing a high degree of
reproducibility in the size of the probes, with the standard deviation variation of the ball lens
diameter being 0.4µm, the ball lens diameter and C-S length being 19µm and 39-42µm
larger than the simulated design, and re-simulation of the beam properties showed that
the SD and WD based on the physical characterization was reasonably close to that of the
selected simulated design. However, due to the uncertainty and experimental error in the
beam profiling, it was not possible validate the beam properties for the prototype probes,
as the experimentally estimated beam properties did not agree with the simulated beam
properties for the prototype probes, why improvements of the needed setup is necessary.
Improvements in the fabrication should be made in order to decrease the attenuation of
the probes, avoid astigmatism, which was observed in 2 out of the 4 prototype probes,
and increase the accuracy of size of the ball lens diameter and C-S length, before moving
forward with the realization of the concept design.
80

A.1
Probe Model Design for Proof of Principle
Figure A.1: A comparison of the probe model design with the probe parameters (top), the
probe model design with the ray matrices (middle), and the probe parameters of the ball
lens interface in ’FPS 1.9.1’. Note that ncf1 is the first ray matrix in the model, and is planar
surface interface between SMF and NCF, while medium1 is curved surface interface ray
matrix. Likewise, both ncf2 and medium2 is both ray matrices, describing the propagation
of the beam through the respective media.

  1 % *********************************
  2 % *      main_forward_facing_balllens     *
  3 % *********************************
  4 % modified from main_NCF_GRIN  v1.0
  5 % Dirk Lorenser, Feb 2015
  6 % adapted/written by Monica M. F. Andresen (s194385), Jan 2025
  7 % 
  8 % for simulating forward-facing NCF ball lens Probes
  9 %
 10 % NOTE: this code uses the physical ray matrices and beam parameters as in
 11 % Kogelnik & Li, Saleh & Teich and Meschede, rather than the reduced ray matrix 
and
 12 % beam parameter system as used in Siegman.
 13 % 
 14 
 15 % Uses normal fiber probe input files for creating matrices M1...M4
 16 % M4 = propagation in sample medium
 17 % M3 = no-core fiber / sample medium interface
 18 % M2 = propagation no-core fiber
 19 % M1 = interface SM fiber / no-core fiber
 20 
 21 clear;
 22 close all;
 23 
 24 mm=1e-3;
 25 um=1e-6;
 26 nm=1e-9;
 27 
 28 probefile='Simple_ForwardFacing_ball_lens';
 29 run(probefile);
 30 
 31 % set geometric = 1 if we want to also do a geometric-optical ray trace
 32 geometric = 0; % default should be 0, because geometric ray trace is misleading in 
this regime
 33 
 34 %% Parameter Ranges
 35 sim_L_ball=50:5:1000;
 36 sim_L_ballradius=75:5:200;
 37 
 38 %Preallocate matrices for storing results
 39 %Define length of matrices
 40 num_L_ball=length(sim_L_ball);
 41 num_L_ballradius=length(sim_L_ballradius);
 42 
 43 %Create matrices to store the results
 44 WD=NaN(num_L_ball,num_L_ballradius);
 45 SD=NaN(num_L_ball,num_L_ballradius);
 46 
 47 %The restraint for allowed working distances
 48 desired_WD=0;%1100; %Set the desired working distance treshold. Note that only 
 49 % working distances higher than the treshold will be stored in the results.
 50 desired_SD_upper=8;%14;% [µm]
 51 desired_SD_lower=6;%12;% [µm]
 52 
 53 %Matrix to sortout excluded designs based (see the related if-statement)
==PAGE:117==
10-04-25 15:33
C:\Users\monic\On...\main_ball_probe_v1.m
2 of 5
 54 Large=NaN(num_L_ball, num_L_ballradius);
 55 
 56 for i=1:num_L_ball
 57     L_NCF=sim_L_ball(i)*um;
 58     for j=1:num_L_ballradius
 59         ballradius=sim_L_ballradius(j)*um;
 60 
 61 %% ----------------------- Calculations --------------------------------
 62 % Here we sequentially apply the individual ray matrices going from
 63 % input to output, and we calculate the complex beam parameter q_i after each
 64 % optical element. Each optical element is stored as a structure. For
 65 % certain elements not all components of the structure make sense, such as
 66 % interfaces with zero length, and in those cases the components are set to
 67 % zero.
 68 % 
 69 % [name].z1         z coordinate at the input of the optical element
 70 % [name].L          length of the optical element
 71 % [name].n          refractive index in the element (if applicable, otherwise 
zero)
 72 % [name].M          ray matrix of the optical element
 73 % [name].q          beam parameter q(z1) at start coordinate of the optical 
element
 74 % [name].x          geometric ray tracing ray vector [height angle]
 75 % [name].zvals      z values for plotting (from [name].z1 to [name].z1 + [name].L)
 76 % [name].wvals      w values for plotting
 77 
 78 % build matrices and calculate the complex beam parameter after each element
 79 %**************************************************************************
 80 
 81 % input
 82 %******
 83 z = 0;
 84 q = 1i*pi*(mfd_SMF/2)^2*n_SMF/lambda; % complex beam parameter input
 85 %geometric ray tracing input
 86 gauss_na_SMF = na_SMF*0.82; % convert the manufacturers 5% NA to a 1/e^2 gaussian 
NA
 87 x = [0; gauss_na_SMF/n_SMF]; % geometric ray tracing input (referred to fiber 
refractive index)
 88 % note: normally the NA of the fiber is given for air. Here we are defining
 89 % the input parameters at the input to the fiber end facet, this is why we
 90 % divide by the refracive index of the fiber. To check this is correct imagine if 
the end facet was air,
 91 % then applying the appropriate ABCD matrix for that interface would
 92 % multiply the above angle by n_SMF again, thereby giving us back the fiber
 93 % NA in air.
 94 
 95 %% ncf1 (Interface SMF to NCF)
 96 %****************************
 97 ncf1.z1 = z;
 98 ncf1.L = 0;
 99 ncf1.n = 0;
100 ncf1.q = q;
101 ncf1.x = x;
102 ncf1.M = ABCD_Interface(n_SMF, n_NCF);
103 
==PAGE:118==
10-04-25 15:33
C:\Users\monic\On...\main_ball_probe_v1.m
3 of 5
104 q = propagate(ncf1.M,q); % propagate to next element
105 x = ncf1.M*x;
106 z = z + ncf1.L;
107 
108 % ncf2 (Propagation in NCF section)
109 %Since the ball lens is going to be fabricated by melting the NCF, the
110 %length of this section should include propogation all the way to the far
111 %curves surface of the ball lens - no need to include the first curved
112 %surface. 
113 %**********************************
114 ncf2.z1 = z;
115 ncf2.L = L_NCF;
116 ncf2.n = n_NCF;
117 ncf2.q = q;
118 ncf2.x = x;
119 ncf2.M = ABCD_Propagation(L_NCF);
120 
121 q = propagate(ncf2.M,q); % propagate to next element
122 x = ncf2.M*x;
123 z = z + ncf2.L;
124 
125 % medium1 (Interface ball to medium)
126 %****************************************
127 
128 % tangential
129 medium1.z1 = z;
130 medium1.L = 0;
131 medium1.n = 0;
132 medium1.q = q;
133 medium1.x = x;
134 medium1.M = ABCD_Curved_Interface(n_ball,n_medium,-ballradius);
135 
136 q = propagate(medium1.M,q); % propagate to next element
137 x = medium1.M*x;
138 
139 z = z + medium1.L;
140 
141 % medium2 (Propagation in medium)
142 %********************************
143 
144 % tangential
145 medium2.z1 = z;
146 medium2.L = output_plotrange;
147 medium2.n = n_medium;
148 medium2.q = q;
149 medium2.x = x;
150 medium2.M = ABCD_Propagation(output_plotrange);
151 
152 q = propagate(medium2.M,q); % propagate to next element
153 x = medium2.M*x;
154 
155 z = z + medium2.L;
156 
157 length_total = z;
158 
==PAGE:119==
10-04-25 15:33
C:\Users\monic\On...\main_ball_probe_v1.m
4 of 5
159 %----------------------- Output and Plots ----------------------------
160 
161 % beam propagation (radii) and ray trace (optional)
162     
163     % plot range
164     plotstart = -200*um;
165     plotend = length_total;
166     plotmax = (50 + 10)*um; % core radius must be visible
167         
168     % plot a little bit of SMF at the start
169     %**************************************
170     smf.zvals = linspace(plotstart,0,10);
171     smf.wvals = mfd_SMF/2*ones(1,10);
172     
173     % beam in No-core fiber
174     %**********************
175     ncf2.zvals = linspace(ncf2.z1, ncf2.z1 + ncf2.L, 100);
176     [w0,z0,z] = beamparameters(ncf2.q, ncf2.n, lambda);
177     ncf2.wvals = w0*sqrt(1 + ((ncf2.zvals - (ncf2.z1 - z))./z0).^2);
178     
179     % output beam
180     %************
181     medium2.zvals = linspace(medium2.z1, medium2.z1 + medium2.L, 100);
182     [w0,z0,z] = beamparameters(medium2.q, medium2.n, lambda);
183     medium2.wvals = w0*sqrt(1 + ((medium2.zvals - (medium2.z1 - z))./z0).^2);
184     
185     
186 
187 
188 %------------ Text Output -----------------------
189 
190 output_w0 = w0;
191 working_distance = -z;
192 rayleigh_range = z0;
193 output_NA = n_medium*sin(lambda/(n_medium*pi*output_w0));
194 
195 SD(i,j)=2*w0/um;
196 WD(i,j)=working_distance/um;
197 
198 if max(ncf2.wvals)>ballradius
199     Large(i,j)=0;
200 else
201     Large(i,j)=1;
202 end
203 
204 
205     end
206 end
207 % Create logical masks for valid designs (WD > 0 for both planes)
208 valid_WD=WD>desired_WD;
209 valid_SD= SD<desired_SD_upper & SD>desired_SD_lower;
210 valid_designs=valid_WD & valid_SD & Large;
211 
212 % Extract the indices of the optimal designs
213 [opt_i, opt_j] = ind2sub(size(WD), find(valid_designs));

1 of 14
  1 % *****************************************************
  2 % *     main_side_facing_balllens    *
  3 % *****************************************************
  4 % modified from main_side_facing  and man_forward_facing_balllens
  5 
  6 %%
  7 % adapted by: Monica M. F. Andresen (s194385), October 2024
  8 %
  9 % main program for ray matrix simulation of side-firing probe consisting of
 10 % NCF, grin fiber and a ball lens that is connected to a SMF and which use 
 11 % a 90 degree reflecting mirror to obtain a side-facing probe. 
 12 % The 90 degree reflection out of the capillary requires that we
 13 % split our calculation into tangential and sagittal planes. See "Modern
 14 % Optical Engineering" by Warren J. Smith for a definition of tangential
 15 % and sagittal. In our case:
 16 % tangential = the plane which contains the optical axis before and after 
reflection,
 17 %              (in cyl. coordinates, a z-r plane obtained by holding phi=const.)
 18 % sagittal = the plane which is obtained by cutting a cross-section through
 19 %               the probe at the point where the beam is reflected off the mirror 
 20 %              (in cyl. coordinates, a r-phi plane obtained by holding z=const.) 
 21 %
 22 % Uses normal fiber probe input files for creating matrices M1...M8 and
 23 % appends matrices M9 ... M12 to the system
 24 % 
 25 % ----------- added optical elements sagittal (s) ----------------
 26 % M14 = propagation in sample medium
 27 % M13 = curved interface capillary outer wall / sample medium
 28 % M12 = propagation in capillary tube
 29 % M11 = air / capillary wall
 30 % ----------- added optical elements tangential (t) --------------
 31 % M14 = propagation in sample medium
 32 % M13 = interface capillary outer wall / sample medium
 33 % M12 = propagation in capillary wall
 34 % M11 = air / capillary tube
 35 % ----------- standard probe  ------------------------------------
 36 % M10 = propagation air
 37 % M9 = ball lens / air interface
 38 % M8 = progagation ball lens
 39 % M7 = interface ncf /ball lens
 40 % M6 = propagation ncf fiber
 41 % M5 = interface GRIN fiber / ncf
 42 % M4 = propagation GRIN fiber
 43 % M3 = no-core fiber / GRIN fiber interface
 44 % M2 = propagation no-core fiber
 45 % M1 = interface SM fiber / no-core fiber
 46 %
 47 % NOTE: this code uses the physical ray matrices and beam parameters as in
 48 % Kogelnik & Li, Saleh & Teich and Meschede, rather than the reduced ray matrix 
and
 49 % beam parameter system as used in Siegman.
 50 
 51 clear;
 52 close all;
 53 
==PAGE:123==

 54 mm = 1e-3;
 55 um = 1e-6;
 56 nm = 1e-9;

 58 %% ------------------------ User input data ----------------------------
 59 
 60 % probe parameters must be defined in an external ".m" file
 61 % see end of this file for an example of a probe definition
 62 
 63 % filename WITHOUT .m extension
 64  probefile='SideFacing_GRIN_Balllens_v8';
 65 run(probefile);
 66 
 67 % probe parameters after GRIN end facet
 68 % *************************************
 69 % Parameters of Glass Capillary Tubing
 70 % index of Vitrocom borosilicate glass (DURAN) = 1.47 at 800nm
 71 % --- settings for "in-capillary" designs -------------
 72 t_capillary = (D2_capillary - D1_capillary)/2; % wall thickness of capillary
 73 endfacet_to_mirror = L_to_mirror; % distance from end of ball lens to mirror
 74 % -----------------------------------------------------
 75 % index and path length of in air
 76 % n_mirror = 1; %refractive index of air
 77 L_mirror = endfacet_to_mirror + D1_capillary/2; % ... plus the capillary inner 
radius
 78 
 79 
 80 % set geometric = 1 if we want to also do a geometric-optical ray trace
 81 geometric = 0; % default should be 0, because geometric ray trace is misleading in 
this regime
 82 
 83 %% Parameter Ranges
 84 sim_L_NCF=50:50:500;  % Range for L_NCF (No-core fiber length)
 85 sim_L_ball=50:50:500; % Range for L_ball (No-core fiber length)
 86 sim_L_GRIN=50:50:1000; % Range for L_GRIN (Graded index fiber length)
 87 sim_L_ballradius=50:50:1000; % Range for L_ballradius (Ball lens radius)
 88 
 89 
 90 % Preallocate 4D matrices for storing results
 91 %Define length of matrices
 92 num_L_NCF=length(sim_L_NCF);
 93 num_L_ball=length(sim_L_ball);
 94 num_L_GRIN=length(sim_L_GRIN);
 95 num_ballradius=length(sim_L_ballradius);
 96 
 97 %Create matrices to store the results
 98 WD_t = NaN(num_L_NCF, num_L_GRIN, num_L_ball, num_ballradius);
 99 WD_s = NaN(num_L_NCF, num_L_GRIN, num_L_ball, num_ballradius);
100 Spot_t = NaN(num_L_NCF, num_L_GRIN, num_L_ball, num_ballradius);
101 Spot_s = NaN(num_L_NCF, num_L_GRIN, num_L_ball, num_ballradius);
102 
103 % The restraint for allowed working distances
104 desired_WD=0; %Set the desired working distance treshold. Note that only 
105 % working distances higher than the treshold will be stored in the results.
106

107 %Matrix to sortout excluded designs based (see the related if-statement)

110 valid_idx=NaN(1,4);
111 m=1;

113 % Simulate all the combinations of the four parameters
114 for i=1:num_L_NCF
115     L_NCF=sim_L_NCF(i)*um;
116     for j=1:num_L_GRIN
117         L_GRIN=sim_L_GRIN(j)*um;
118         for k=1:num_L_ball
119             L_ball=sim_L_ball(k)*um;
120             for l=1:num_ballradius
121                 ballradius=sim_L_ballradius(l)*um;
122 
123 
124 %% ----------------------- Calculations --------------------------------
125 % Here we sequentially apply the individual ray matrices going from
126 % input to output, and we calculate the complex beam parameter q_i after each
127 % optical element. Each optical element is stored as a structure. For
128 % certain elements not all components of the structure make sense, such as
129 % interfaces with zero length, and in those cases the components are set to
130 % zero.
131 % 
132 % [name].z1         z coordinate at the input of the optical element
133 % [name].L          length of the optical element
134 % [name].n          refractive index in the element (if applicable, otherwise 
zero)
135 % [name].M          ray matrix of the optical element
136 % [name].q          beam parameter q(z1) at start coordinate of the optical 
element
137 % [name].x          geometric ray tracing ray vector [height angle]
138 % [name].zvals      z values for plotting (from [name].z1 to [name].z1 + [name].L)
139 % [name].wvals      w values for plotting
140 
141 % build matrices and calculate the complex beam parameter after each element
142 %**************************************************************************
143 
144 % input
145 %******
146 z = 0;
147 q = 1i*pi*(mfd_SMF/2)^2*n_SMF/lambda; % complex beam parameter input
148 %geometric ray tracing input
149 gauss_na_SMF = na_SMF*0.82; % convert the manufacturers 5% NA to a 1/e^2 gaussian 
NA
150 x = [0; gauss_na_SMF/n_SMF]; % geometric ray tracing input (referred to fiber 
refractive index)
151 % note: normally the NA of the fiber is given for air. Here we are defining
152 % the input parameters at the input to the fiber end facet, this is why we
153 % divide by the refracive index of the fiber. To check this is correct imagine if 
the end facet was air,
154 % then applying the appropriate ABCD matrix for that interface would
155 % multiply the above angle by n_SMF again, thereby giving us back the fiber
156 % NA in air.
==PAGE:125==

158 %% ncf1 (Interface SMF to NCF)
159 %****************************
160 ncf1.z1 = z;
161 ncf1.L = 0;
162 ncf1.n = 0;
163 ncf1.q = q;
164 ncf1.x = x;
165 ncf1.M = ABCD_Interface(n_SMF, n_NCF);
166 
167 q = propagate(ncf1.M,q); % propagate to next element
168 x = ncf1.M*x;
169 z = z + ncf1.L;
170 
171 %% ncf2 (Propagation in NFC section)
172 %**********************************
173 ncf2.z1 = z;
174 ncf2.L = L_NCF;
175 ncf2.n = n_NCF;
176 ncf2.q = q;
177 ncf2.x = x;
178 ncf2.M = ABCD_Propagation(L_NCF);
179 
180 q = propagate(ncf2.M,q); % propagate to next element
181 x = ncf2.M*x;
182 z = z+ncf2.L;
183 
184 %% grin1 (Interface NCF to GRIN)
185 %*****************************
186 grin1.z1 = z;
187 grin1.L = 0;
188 grin1.n = 0;
189 grin1.q = q;
190 grin1.x = x;
191 grin1.M = ABCD_Interface(n_NCF, n_0);
192 
193 q = propagate(grin1.M,q); % propagate to next element
194 x = grin1.M*x;
195 z = z + grin1.L;
196 
197 %% grin2 (Propagation in GRIN section)
198 %************************************
199 grin2.z1 = z;

201 grin2.n = n_0;

203 grin2.x = x;
204 grin2.M = ABCD_GRIN(g, L_GRIN);
205 
206 q = propagate(grin2.M,q); % propagate to next element
207 x = grin2.M*x;
208 z = z + grin2.L;

1 of 11
  1 % *****************************************************
  2 % *     concept_plot    *
  3 % *****************************************************
  4 % modified from main_side_facing  and man_forward_facing_balllens
  5 
  6 %%
  7 % adapted by: Monica M. F. Andresen (s194385), April 2024
  8 %
  9 % main program for ray matrix simulation of side-firing probe consisting of
 10 % NCF, grin fiber and a ball lens that is connected to a SMF and which use 
 11 % a 90 degree reflecting mirror to obtain a side-facing probe. 
 12 % The 90 degree reflection out of the capillary requires that we
 13 % split our calculation into tangential and sagittal planes. See "Modern
 14 % Optical Engineering" by Warren J. Smith for a definition of tangential
 15 % and sagittal. In our case:
 16 % tangential = the plane which contains the optical axis before and after 
reflection,
 17 %              (in cyl. coordinates, a z-r plane obtained by holding phi=const.)
 18 % sagittal = the plane which is obtained by cutting a cross-section through
 19 %               the probe at the point where the beam is reflected off the mirror 
 20 %              (in cyl. coordinates, a r-phi plane obtained by holding z=const.) 
 21 %
 22 % Uses normal fiber probe input files for creating matrices M1...M8 and
 23 % appends matrices M9 ... M12 to the system
 24 % 
 25 % ----------- added optical elements sagittal (s) ----------------
 26 % M14 = propagation in sample medium
 27 % M13 = curved interface capillary outer wall / sample medium
 28 % M12 = propagation in capillary tube
 29 % M11 = air / capillary wall
 30 % ----------- added optical elements tangential (t) --------------
 31 % M14 = propagation in sample medium
 32 % M13 = interface capillary outer wall / sample medium
 33 % M12 = propagation in capillary wall
 34 % M11 = air / capillary tube
 35 % ----------- standard probe  ------------------------------------
 36 % M10 = propagation air
 37 % M9 = ball lens / air interface
 38 % M8 = progagation ball lens
 39 % M7 = interface ncf /ball lens
 40 % M6 = propagation ncf fiber
 41 % M5 = interface GRIN fiber / ncf
 42 % M4 = propagation GRIN fiber
 43 % M3 = no-core fiber / GRIN fiber interface
 44 % M2 = propagation no-core fiber
 45 % M1 = interface SM fiber / no-core fiber
 46 %
 47 % NOTE: this code uses the physical ray matrices and beam parameters as in
 48 % Kogelnik & Li, Saleh & Teich and Meschede, rather than the reduced ray matrix 
and
 49 % beam parameter system as used in Siegman.
 50 
 51 clear;
 52 close all;
 53 
==PAGE:138==
10-04-25 15:31

 54 mm = 1e-3;
 55 um = 1e-6;
 56 nm = 1e-9;

 58 %% ------------------------ User input data ----------------------------
 59 
 60 % probe parameters must be defined in an external ".m" file
 61 % see end of this file for an example of a probe definition
 62 
 63 % filename WITHOUT .m extension
 64  probefile='SideFacing_GRIN_Balllens_v8';
 65 run(probefile);
 66 
 67 % probe parameters after GRIN end facet
 68 % *************************************
 69 % Parameters of Glass Capillary Tubing
 70 % index of Vitrocom borosilicate glass (DURAN) = 1.47 at 800nm
 71 % --- settings for "in-capillary" designs -------------
 72 t_capillary = (D2_capillary - D1_capillary)/2; % wall thickness of capillary
 73 endfacet_to_mirror = L_to_mirror; % distance from end of ball lens to mirror
 74 % -----------------------------------------------------
 75 % index and path length of in air
 76 % n_mirror = 1; %refractive index of air
 77 L_mirror = endfacet_to_mirror + D1_capillary/2; % ... plus the capillary inner 
radius
 78 
 79 
 80 % set geometric = 1 if we want to also do a geometric-optical ray trace
 81 geometric = 0; % default should be 0, because geometric ray trace is misleading in 
this regime
 82 
 83 
 84 
 85 %% ----------------------- Calculations --------------------------------
 86 % Here we sequentially apply the individual ray matrices going from
 87 % input to output, and we calculate the complex beam parameter q_i after each
 88 % optical element. Each optical element is stored as a structure. For
 89 % certain elements not all components of the structure make sense, such as
 90 % interfaces with zero length, and in those cases the components are set to
 91 % zero.
 92 % 
 93 % [name].z1         z coordinate at the input of the optical element
 94 % [name].L          length of the optical element
 95 % [name].n          refractive index in the element (if applicable, otherwise 
zero)
 96 % [name].M          ray matrix of the optical element
 97 % [name].q          beam parameter q(z1) at start coordinate of the optical 
element
 98 % [name].x          geometric ray tracing ray vector [height angle]
 99 % [name].zvals      z values for plotting (from [name].z1 to [name].z1 + [name].L)
100 % [name].wvals      w values for plotting
101 
102 % build matrices and calculate the complex beam parameter after each element
103 %**************************************************************************
104 
==PAGE:139==

105 % input
106 %******
107 z = 0;
108 q = 1i*pi*(mfd_SMF/2)^2*n_SMF/lambda; % complex beam parameter input
109 %geometric ray tracing input
110 gauss_na_SMF = na_SMF*0.82; % convert the manufacturers 5% NA to a 1/e^2 gaussian 
NA
111 x = [0; gauss_na_SMF/n_SMF]; % geometric ray tracing input (referred to fiber 
refractive index)
112 % note: normally the NA of the fiber is given for air. Here we are defining
113 % the input parameters at the input to the fiber end facet, this is why we
114 % divide by the refracive index of the fiber. To check this is correct imagine if 
the end facet was air,
115 % then applying the appropriate ABCD matrix for that interface would
116 % multiply the above angle by n_SMF again, thereby giving us back the fiber
117 % NA in air.
118 
119 %% ncf1 (Interface SMF to NCF)
120 %****************************
121 ncf1.z1 = z;
122 ncf1.L = 0;
123 ncf1.n = 0;
124 ncf1.q = q;
125 ncf1.x = x;
126 ncf1.M = ABCD_Interface(n_SMF, n_NCF);
127 
128 q = propagate(ncf1.M,q); % propagate to next element
129 x = ncf1.M*x;
130 z = z + ncf1.L;
131 
132 %% ncf2 (Propagation in NFC section)
133 %**********************************
134 ncf2.z1 = z;
135 ncf2.L = L_NCF;
136 ncf2.n = n_NCF;
137 ncf2.q = q;
138 ncf2.x = x;
139 ncf2.M = ABCD_Propagation(L_NCF);
140 
141 q = propagate(ncf2.M,q); % propagate to next element
142 x = ncf2.M*x;
143 z = z+ncf2.L;
144 
145 %% grin1 (Interface NCF to GRIN)
146 %*****************************
147 grin1.z1 = z;
148 grin1.L = 0;
149 grin1.n = 0;
150 grin1.q = q;
151 grin1.x = x;
152 grin1.M = ABCD_Interface(n_NCF, n_0);
153

155 x = grin1.M*x;
156 z = z + grin1.L;

412 for p = 1:chop
413     [w0,z0,z] = beamparameters(q, grin2.n, lambda);
414     grin2.wvals(p) = w0*sqrt(1 + (z/z0)^2);

%% exponential regression function
17
function [p, CI, rsq]=expPlot(BallDiameterum , ActualDiametermumX
,idx, idx_reg ,strx,stry,ftitle ,fno, myfigure)
18
% Calculate the exponential regression
19
f=fit(BallDiameterum(idx_reg),ActualDiametermumX(idx_reg),'exp1'
);
20
p=coeffvalues(f);
21
CI=confint(f);
22
23
%calculate R^2
24
fresid=ActualDiametermumX(idx_reg)-(p(1)*exp(p(2)*BallDiameterum
(idx_reg)));
25
SSresid=sum(fresid.^2);
26
SStotal=(length(ActualDiametermumX(idx_reg))-1)*var(
ActualDiametermumX(idx_reg));
27
rsq=1-SSresid/SStotal;
28
29
30
31
%%
32

33

)

B.2.2.1
regrPlot.m
1
function regrPlot(X,Y,idx,idx_reg ,xLabel,yLabel,fTitle,fno,
myfigure)
2
% sort data
3
XYs=sortrows([X(idx_reg) Y(idx_reg)],1);
4
Xs=XYs(:,1);
5
Ys=XYs(:,2);
6
7
figure(fno);
8
9
if sum(idx)~=0
10
scatter(X(idx),Y(idx),100,'filled','o');
11
end
12
hold on
13
grid on
14
grid minor
15
16
17
%Linear reggression for
18
% p=polyfit(Xs,Ys,1);
19
% f=polyval(p,Xs);
20
f=fit(Xs,Ys,'poly1');
21
p=coeffvalues(f);
22
CI=confint(f,0.95);
23
24
plot(f,'k--') %
25
legend off
26
hold on
27
28
29
%
30
%calculate R^2
31
fresid=Ys-(p(1)*Xs+p(2));
32
SSresid=sum(fresid.^2);
33
SStotal=(length(Ys)-1)*var(Ys);
34
rsq=1-SSresid/SStotal;
35
% Add eqation and R^2 to the plot
36
txt1=['Linear :
' num2str(p(1)) ' $\cdot$ x ' num2str(p(2))];
37
txt2=['R$^{2}$ :
' num2str(rsq)];
38
txt={txt1,txt2};
39
text(0.05,0.9, txt, 'Units','normalized','Interpreter','latex')
40
41
% Plot 95% confidence interval
42
% Calculate the linear curves
43
lin_x=linspace(min(Xs),max(Xs) ,1000);
44
lowerCI=CI(1,1)*lin_x+CI(1,2);
45
upperCI=CI(2,1)*lin_x+CI(2,2);
46
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
161
==PAGE:176==
47
hold on
48
plot(lin_x,lowerCI ,'r')
49
plot(lin_x,upperCI ,'r')
50
xlabel(xLabel ,'Interpreter','latex')
51
ylabel(yLabel ,'Interpreter','latex')
52
title(fTitle)
53
54
%% Making the figures ready for use out side of matlab
55
hfig = figure(fno);
% save the figure handle in a variable
56
fname = myfigure; % To save figure to
57
58
picturewidth = 25;%20; % set this parameter and keep it forever
59
hw_ratio = 0.75; % feel free to play with this ratio
60
set(findall(hfig,'-property','FontSize'),'FontSize',21) % adjust
fontsize to your document
61
62
63
set(findall(hfig,'-property','Box'),'Box','off') % optional
64
set(findall(hfig,'-property','Interpreter'),'Interpreter','latex
')

B.2.2.2
fwhm.m
1
function [maxvalue ,halfMaxValue ,fwhm_value]=fwhm(x,optionstr)
2
3
if strcmp(optionstr ,'raw')
4
maxvalue=max(x(:,2));
5
halfMaxValue = max(x(:,2)) / 2; % Find the half max value.
6
% Find indexes of power where the power first and last is above
the half max value.
7
leftIndex = find(x(:,2) >= halfMaxValue , 1, 'first');
8
rightIndex = find(x(:,2) >= halfMaxValue , 1, 'last');
9
% Compute the delta time value by using those indexes in the
time vector.
10
fwhm_value = x(rightIndex ,1) - x(leftIndex ,1);
11
elseif strcmp(optionstr ,'gauss')
12
maxvalue=max(x(:,4));
13
halfMaxValue = max(x(:,4)) / 2; % Find the half max value.
14
% Find indexes of power where the power first and last is above
the half max value.
15
leftIndex = find(x(:,4) >= halfMaxValue , 1, 'first');
16
rightIndex = find(x(:,4) >= halfMaxValue , 1, 'last');
17
% Compute the delta time value by using those indexes in the
time vector.
18
fwhm_value = x(rightIndex ,3) - x(leftIndex ,3);
19
end
20
21
22
23
end

%% ax=retrieveAxis(probe_struct)
2
%
Calculates the distance for each of the points acquired.
3
%
probe_struct , should be of the structure created with
grabdata.m
4
%
5
%
In case, that probe_struct.key.startDist_exp does not
contain a value,
6
%
the user will be prompted to give an input
7
% written by Monica M. F. Andresen (s194385) 14/3-2025
8
function ax=retrieveAxis(probe_struct ,opts,idx)
9
fwhmx=probe_struct.fwhmx(idx);
10
incr=probe_struct.key.incr(idx);
11
12
if strcmp(opts,'experiment')
13
if isnan(probe_struct.key.startDist_exp)
14
ax=str2double(input('Define the start distance for
','s
'))*10^4;
15
else
16
ax=probe_struct.key.startDist_exp*10^4;
17
end
18
for ii=2:(numel(fwhmx))
19
ax(ii)=ax(ii-1)-(incr(ii)*10);
20
21
end
22
elseif strcmp(opts,'analysis')
23
ax=probe_struct.key.startDist_meas*10^4;
24
for ii=2:(numel(fwhmx))
25
ax(ii)=ax(ii-1)-(incr(ii)*10);
26
27
end
28
29
elseif strcmp(opts,'hit')
30
ax=(1:numel(fwhmx))*0;
31
ax(numel(fwhmx))=(0.9-0.44)*10^4;
32
33
for ii=1:(numel(ax)-1)
34
ax(end-ii)=ax(end-ii+1)+(incr(end-ii+1)*10);
35
36
end
37
38
end
39
end

B.2.2.5
beamprofile_fit.m
1
%% [xyfit, gof, output]=beamprofile_fit(probe_struct , ax, idx)
2
%
Calculates the distance for each of the points acquired.
3
%
probe_struct , should be of the structure created with
grabdata.m
4
%
ax, is the axis calculated with retrieveAxis.m
5
%
idx, the index of the fwhm that should be included in the
fit
6
%
7
%
xyfit, is a struct containing
8
%
.x.coef, estimated w0 and x0 for probe_struct.fwhmx
9
%
.y.coef, estimated w0 and x0 for probe_struct.fwhmy
10
%
.x.ci, the 95% confidence interval for the estiamted
w0 and x0
11
%
.y.ci, the 95% confidence interval for the estiamted
w0 and x0
12
%
.x.curve, the curve of the fitted model based on
13
%
probe_struct.fwhmx
14
%
.y.curve, the curve of the fitted model based on
15
%
probe_struct.fwhmy
16
%
.x.cci, the curves of the 95% confidence interval of
the fitted
17
%
model
18
%
.y.cci, the curves of the 95% confidence interval of
the fitted
19
%
model
20
%
21
%
x0, is the estimated working distance
22
%
w0, is the estimated spot diameter at the focal point
23
%
24
% written by Monica M. F. Andresen (s194385) 27/3-2025
25
function [xyfit, gof, output]=beamprofile_fit(data,ax,idx)
26
% model to fit the data to. Modified beam waist equartion
27
mdl=fittype('(w/2)*sqrt(1 + ((0.830*(x-x0))/(3.14*((w/2)^2))
)^2)','coeff',{'w','x0'});
28
29
% define start points
30
startpoints=[13, 1500];
31
32
%select the datapoint to be included in the fit
33
wdx=data.fwhmx(idx);
34
wdy=data.fwhmy(idx);
35
% Fit the model
36
[xyfitx , gof.x, output.x]=fit(ax',(wdx)'/2,mdl,'Start',
startpoints);
37
[xyfity , gof.y, output.y]=fit(ax',(wdy)'/2,mdl,'Start',
startpoints);
38
39
%save the estiamted coefficient
A Miniaturized Fiber-Optic Probe for Optical Coherence Tomography imaging in Endoscopy
167
==PAGE:182==
40
xyfit.x.coef=coeffvalues(xyfitx);
41
xyfit.y.coef=coeffvalues(xyfity);
42
43
% save the CI
44
xyfit.x.ci=confint(xyfitx);
45
xyfit.y.ci=confint(xyfity);
46
47
% define axis for curve
48
xyfit.x.curve_ax=-100:10:max(ax);
49
xyfit.y.curve_ax=xyfit.x.curve_ax;
50
cax=xyfit.x.curve_ax;
51
52
% compute the curve for the fitted model
53
xyfit.x.curve=2* ((xyfit.x.coef(1)/2).*sqrt(1 + ((0.830*(cax-
xyfit.x.coef(2)))./(3.14*((xyfit.x.coef(1)/2).^2))).^2));
54
xyfit.y.curve=2* ((xyfit.y.coef(1)/2).*sqrt(1 + ((0.830*(cax-
xyfit.y.coef(2)))./(3.14*((xyfit.y.coef(1)/2).^2))).^2));
55
56
% compute the curve for the CI for the fitted model
57
xyfit.x.cci=2* ((xyfit.x.ci(:,1)/2).*sqrt(1 + ((0.830*(cax-
xyfit.x.coef(2)))./(3.14*((xyfit.x.ci(:,1)/2).^2))).^2));
58
xyfit.y.cci=2* ((xyfit.y.ci(:,1)/2).*sqrt(1 + ((0.830*(cax-
xyfit.y.coef(2)))./(3.14*((xyfit.y.ci(:,1)/2).^2))).^2));
59
60
end

10
11
12

13

14

15
16
